<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">History of music</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>

<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>

<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

<p>Although <a href="/wiki/Definition_of_music" title="Definition of music">definitions of music</a> vary wildly throughout the world, every known <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">culture</a> partakes in it, and music is thus considered a <a href="/wiki/Cultural_universal" title="Cultural universal">cultural universal</a>. The <a href="#Origins">origins of music</a> remain highly contentious; commentators often relate it to the <a href="/wiki/Origin_of_language" title="Origin of language">origin of language</a>, with much disagreement surrounding whether music arose before, after or simultaneously with <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a>. Many theories have been proposed by scholars from a wide range of disciplines, though none have achieved wide approval. Most cultures have their own <a href="/wiki/Origin_myth" title="Origin myth">mythical origins</a> concerning the invention of music, generally rooted in their respective mythological, religious or philosophical beliefs.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_music" title="Prehistoric music">music of prehistoric cultures</a> is first firmly dated to <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 40,000</span> <a href="/wiki/Before_Present" title="Before Present">BP</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Paleolithic" title="Upper Paleolithic">Upper Paleolithic</a> by evidence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bone_flutes" title="Bone flutes">bone flutes</a>, though it remains unclear whether or not the actual origins lie in the earlier <a href="/wiki/Middle_Paleolithic" title="Middle Paleolithic">Middle Paleolithic</a> period (300,000 to 50,000 BP). There is little known about prehistoric music, with traces mainly limited to some simple flutes and percussion instruments. However, such evidence indicates that music existed to some extent in prehistoric societies such as the <a href="/wiki/Xia_dynasty" title="Xia dynasty">Xia dynasty</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_civilisation" title="Indus Valley civilisation">Indus Valley civilisation</a>. Upon the development of writing, the music of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Literate" title="Literate">literate</a> civilizations—<a href="/wiki/Ancient_music" title="Ancient music">ancient music</a>—was present in the major Chinese, Egyptian, Greek, Indian, Persian, Mesopotamian, and Middle Eastern societies. It is difficult to make many generalizations about ancient music as a whole, but from what is known it was often characterized by <a href="/wiki/Monophony" title="Monophony">monophony</a> and <a href="/wiki/Improvisation" title="Improvisation">improvisation</a>. In ancient <a href="/wiki/Song" title="Song">song</a> forms, the texts were closely aligned with music, and though the oldest extant musical notation survives from this period, many texts survive without their accompanying music, such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">Rigveda</a></i> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shijing" title="Shijing"><i>Shijing</i> Classic of Poetry</a>. The eventual emergence of the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> and increasing contact between cultures led to the transmission and exchange of musical ideas, practices, and instruments. Such interaction led to the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a>'s music being heavily influenced by Central Asian traditions, while the Tang dynasty's music, the Japanese <i><a href="/wiki/Gagaku" title="Gagaku">gagaku</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Korean_court_music" title="Korean court music">Korean court music</a> each influenced each other.
</p><p>Historically, religions have often been catalysts for music. The <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> immensely influenced <a href="/wiki/Indian_classical_music" title="Indian classical music">Indian classical music</a>, while the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_Classics" title="Five Classics">Five Classics</a> of <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a> laid the basis for subsequent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_music" title="Chinese music">Chinese music</a>. Following the rapid <a href="/wiki/Spread_of_Islam" title="Spread of Islam">spread of Islam</a> in the 6th century, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_music" title="Islamic music">Islamic music</a> dominated Persia and the Arab world, and the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Golden Age</a> saw the presence of numerous important music theorists. Music written for and by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Christian_Church" title="Early Christian Church">early Christian Church</a> properly inaugurates the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_classical_music" title="Western classical music">Western classical music</a> tradition,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrout19732_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrout19732-1">[1]</a></sup> which continues into <a href="/wiki/Medieval_music" title="Medieval music">medieval music</a> where <a href="/wiki/Polyphony" title="Polyphony">polyphony</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Staff_notation" title="Staff notation">staff notation</a> and nascent forms of many modern instruments developed. In addition to religion or the lack thereof, a society's music is influenced by all other aspects of its culture, including social and economic organization and experience, climate, and access to technology. Many cultures have coupled music with other art forms, such as the Chinese <a href="/wiki/Four_arts" title="Four arts">four arts</a> and the medieval <i><a href="/wiki/Quadrivium" title="Quadrivium">quadrivium</a></i>. The emotions and ideas that music expresses, the situations in which music is played and listened to, and the attitudes toward <a href="/wiki/Musician" title="Musician">musicians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Composer" title="Composer">composers</a> all vary between regions and periods. Many cultures have or continue to distinguish between <a href="/wiki/Art_music" title="Art music">art music</a> (or 'classical music'), <a href="/wiki/Folk_music" title="Folk music">folk music</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Popular_music" title="Popular music">popular music</a>.
</p>

<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9"><a href="#Persia_and_Mesopotamia"><span class="tocnumber">3.4</span> <span class="toctext">Persia and Mesopotamia</span></a>
<ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_musicology#The_origins_of_music" title="Evolutionary musicology">Evolutionary musicology § The origins of music</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Music_archaeology" title="Music archaeology">Music archaeology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Definition_of_music" title="Definition of music">Definition of music</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:24em; ; background-color: #c6dbf7;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>"But that music is a language by whose means messages are elaborated, that such messages can be understood by the many but sent out only by the few, and that it alone among all language unites the contradictory character of being at once intelligible and untranslatable—these facts make the creator of music a being like the gods and make music itself the supreme mystery of human knowledge."
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style=""><a href="/wiki/Claude_L%C3%A9vi-Strauss" title="Claude Lévi-Strauss">Claude Lévi-Strauss</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Raw_and_the_Cooked" title="The Raw and the Cooked">The Raw and the Cooked</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Music" title="Music">Music</a> is regarded as a <a href="/wiki/Cultural_universal" title="Cultural universal">cultural universal</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMithen200512_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMithen200512-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorley20135_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorley20135-4">[4]</a></sup> though <a href="/wiki/Definition_of_music" title="Definition of music">definitions</a> of it vary wildly around the world and throughout history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMithen200526–27_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMithen200526–27-5">[5]</a></sup> As with many aspects of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_cognition" title="Human cognition">human cognition</a>, it remains debated as to what extent the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Origins_of_music" title="Origins of music">origins of music</a> will ever be understood, with scholars often taking polarizing positions.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMerkerMorleyZuidema2015"Introduction"_6-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMerkerMorleyZuidema2015"Introduction"-6'>[6]</a></sup> The origin of music is often discussed alongside the <a href="/wiki/Origin_of_language" title="Origin of language">origin of language</a>, with the nature of their connection being the subject of much debate.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldstein2016"Language_and_Music_in_Cognition"_7-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldstein2016"Language_and_Music_in_Cognition"-7'>[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[n 1]</a></sup> However, before the mid-late 20th century, both topics were seldom given substantial attention by academics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMithen200513_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMithen200513-10">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWallinMerkerBrown20003–4_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWallinMerkerBrown20003–4-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[n 2]</a></sup> Since the topic's resurgence, the principal source of contention is divided into three perspectives: whether music began as a kind of <a href="/wiki/Proto-language" title="Proto-language">proto-language</a> (a result of <a href="/wiki/Adaptation" title="Adaptation">adaptation</a>) that led to language; if music is a <a href="/wiki/Spandrel_(biology)" title="Spandrel (biology)">spandrel</a> (a <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">phenotypic</a> by-product of evolution) that was the result of language; or if music and language both derived from a common antecedent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWallinMerkerBrown20008_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWallinMerkerBrown20008-14">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown2000271_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown2000271-15">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[n 3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[n 4]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is little consensus on any particular theory for the origin of music, which have included contributions from archaeologists, cognitive scientists, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnomusicologists" title="Ethnomusicologists">ethnomusicologists</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evolutionary_biologists" title="Evolutionary biologists">evolutionary biologists</a>, linguists, neuroscientists, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paleoanthropologists" title="Paleoanthropologists">paleoanthropologists</a>, philosophers, and psychologists (<a href="/wiki/Developmental_psychology" title="Developmental psychology">developmental</a> and <a href="/wiki/Social_psychology" title="Social psychology">social</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorley20132–3_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorley20132–3-26">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[n 5]</a></sup> Some of the most prominent theories are as follows:
</p>
<ul><li>Music arose as an elaborate form of <a href="/wiki/Sexual_selection" title="Sexual selection">sexual selection</a>, perhaps arising in mating calls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuron200361_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuron200361-29">[24]</a></sup> This theory, perhaps the first significant one on music's origins,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBickerton2000153_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBickerton2000153-30">[25]</a></sup> is generally credited to <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a>.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTENettl2001"8._On_the_Origins_of_Music"_31-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTENettl2001"8._On_the_Origins_of_Music"-31'>[26]</a></sup> It first appeared in Darwin's 1871 book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Descent_of_Man,_and_Selection_in_Relation_to_Sex" title="The Descent of Man, and Selection in Relation to Sex">The Descent of Man, and Selection in Relation to Sex</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMithen200513_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMithen200513-10">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[27]</a></sup> and has since been criticized as there is no evidence that either human sex is "more musical" thus no evidence of <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">sexual dimorphism</a>; there are currently no other examples of sexual selection that do not include considerable sexual dimorphism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuron200362_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuron200362-33">[28]</a></sup> Recent commentators, citing music's use in other animals's mating systems, have nonetheless propagated and developed Darwin's theory; such scholars include Peter J.B. Slater, <a href="/wiki/Katy_Payne" title="Katy Payne">Katy Payne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bj%C3%B6rn_Merker" title="Björn Merker">Björn Merker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Miller_(psychologist)" title="Geoffrey Miller (psychologist)">Geoffrey Miller</a> and Peter Todd.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWallinMerkerBrown200011_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWallinMerkerBrown200011-34">[29]</a></sup></li>
<li>Music arose alongside language, both of which supposedly descend from a "shared precursor",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorley2013315_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorley2013315-35">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown2000279_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown2000279-36">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWallinMerkerBrown200011_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWallinMerkerBrown200011-34">[29]</a></sup>. The biologist <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Spencer" title="Herbert Spencer">Herbert Spencer</a> was an important early proponent of this theory, as was the composer <a href="/wiki/Richard_Wagner" title="Richard Wagner">Richard Wagner</a>,<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTENettl2001"8._On_the_Origins_of_Music"_31-1'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTENettl2001"8._On_the_Origins_of_Music"-31'>[26]</a></sup> who termed the music and language's shared ancestor as "speech-music".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWallinMerkerBrown20008_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWallinMerkerBrown20008-14">[12]</a></sup> Since the 21st-century, a number of scholars have supported this theory, particularly the archeologist <a href="/wiki/Steven_Mithen" title="Steven Mithen">Steven Mithen</a>.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTENettl2001"8._On_the_Origins_of_Music"_31-2'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTENettl2001"8._On_the_Origins_of_Music"-31'>[26]</a></sup></li>
<li>Music arose to fulfill a practical need. Propositions include:
<ul><li>To assist in organizing cohesive labor, first proposed by the economist <a href="/wiki/Karl_B%C3%BCcher" title="Karl Bücher">Karl Bücher</a>.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTENettl2001"8._On_the_Origins_of_Music"_31-3'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTENettl2001"8._On_the_Origins_of_Music"-31'>[26]</a></sup></li>
<li>To improve the ease and range of long distance communication, first proposed by the musicologist <a href="/wiki/Carl_Stumpf" title="Carl Stumpf">Carl Stumpf</a>.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTENettl2001"8._On_the_Origins_of_Music"_31-4'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTENettl2001"8._On_the_Origins_of_Music"-31'>[26]</a></sup></li>
<li>To enhance communication with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Divine" title="Divine">divine</a> or otherwise <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a>, first proposed by the anthropologist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siegfried_Nadel" title="Siegfried Nadel">Siegfried Nadel</a>.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTENettl2001"8._On_the_Origins_of_Music"_31-5'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTENettl2001"8._On_the_Origins_of_Music"-31'>[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENadel1930_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENadel1930-37">[32]</a></sup></li>
<li>To assist in "coordination, cohesion and cooperation", particularly in the context of families or communities.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTENettl2001"8._On_the_Origins_of_Music"_31-6'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTENettl2001"8._On_the_Origins_of_Music"-31'>[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWallinMerkerBrown200011_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWallinMerkerBrown200011-34">[29]</a></sup></li>
<li>To be a means for frightening off predators or enemies of some kind.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTENettl2001"8._On_the_Origins_of_Music"_31-7'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTENettl2001"8._On_the_Origins_of_Music"-31'>[26]</a></sup></li></ul></li>
<li>Music had two origins, "from speech (logogenic) and from emotional expression (pathogenic)", first proposed by the musicologist <a href="/wiki/Curt_Sachs" title="Curt Sachs">Curt Sachs</a>. Reflecting on the diversity of music around the world, Sachs noted that some music confines to either a communicative or expressionistic form, suggesting that these aspects developed separately.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTENettl2001"8._On_the_Origins_of_Music"_31-8'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTENettl2001"8._On_the_Origins_of_Music"-31'>[26]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Many cultures have their own <a href="/wiki/Origin_myth" title="Origin myth">mythical origins</a> on the creation of music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuron200357_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuron200357-38">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray202013_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray202013-39">[34]</a></sup> Specific figures are sometimes credited with inventing music, such as <a href="/wiki/Jubal_(Bible)" title="Jubal (Bible)">Jubal</a> in <a href="/wiki/Christian_mythology" title="Christian mythology">Christian mythology</a>,<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTENettl2001"8._On_the_Origins_of_Music"_31-9'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTENettl2001"8._On_the_Origins_of_Music"-31'>[26]</a></sup> the legendary <a href="/wiki/Shah" title="Shah">Shah</a> <a href="/wiki/Jamshid" title="Jamshid">Jamshid</a> in <a href="/wiki/Persian_mythology" title="Persian mythology">Persian/Iranian mythology</a>,<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFarhat2012"Historic_Retrospective"_40-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFarhat2012"Historic_Retrospective"-40'>[35]</a></sup> the goddess <a href="/wiki/Saraswati" title="Saraswati">Saraswati</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[36]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Muses" title="Muses">muses</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_mythology" title="Ancient Greek mythology">Ancient Greek mythology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray202013_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray202013-39">[34]</a></sup> Some cultures credit multiple originators of music; ancient <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_mythology" title="Egyptian mythology">Egyptian mythology</a> associates it with numerous deities, including <a href="/wiki/Amun" title="Amun">Amun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hathor" title="Hathor">Hathor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isis" title="Isis">Isis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Osiris" title="Osiris">Osiris</a>, but especially <a href="/wiki/Ihy" title="Ihy">Ihy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2001_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2001-42">[37]</a></sup> There are many stories relating to music's origins in <a href="/wiki/Chinese_mythology" title="Chinese mythology">Chinese mythology</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFalkenhausen200088_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFalkenhausen200088-43">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[n 6]</a></sup> but the most prominent is that of the musician <a href="/wiki/Ling_Lun" title="Ling Lun">Ling Lun</a>, who—on the orders of the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Emperor" title="Yellow Emperor">Yellow Emperor</a> (Huangdi)—invented <a href="/wiki/Bamboo_flute" title="Bamboo flute">bamboo flute</a> by imitating the song of the mythical <i><a href="/wiki/Fenghuang" title="Fenghuang">fenghuang</a></i> birds.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalm2019"Ancient_artifacts_and_writings"_45-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalm2019"Ancient_artifacts_and_writings"-45'>[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[n 7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Prehistory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_music" title="Prehistoric music">Prehistoric music</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:332px;max-width:332px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:164px;max-width:164px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:242px;overflow:hidden"><a src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Super_alte_Fl%C3%B6te.jpg/162px-Super_alte_Fl%C3%B6te.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Super_alte_Fl%C3%B6te.jpg/243px-Super_alte_Fl%C3%B6te.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Super_alte_Fl%C3%B6te.jpg/324px-Super_alte_Fl%C3%B6te.jpg 2x" width="162"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:330px;max-width:330px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:218px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bone_Flute.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="5304" data-file-width="7952" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Bone_Flute.jpg/328px-Bone_Flute.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Bone_Flute.jpg/492px-Bone_Flute.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Bone_Flute.jpg/656px-Bone_Flute.jpg 2x" width="328"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption"><i>Top left</i>, the purported <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Divje_Babe_Flute" title="Divje Babe Flute">Divje Babe Flute</a> from <a href="/wiki/Divje_Babe" title="Divje Babe">Divje Babe</a>, Slovenia;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorley201338–39_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorley201338–39-47">[40]</a></sup> <i>top right</i> a <a href="/wiki/Aurignacian" title="Aurignacian">Aurignacian</a> <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic_flutes" title="Paleolithic flutes">bone flute</a> from <a href="/wiki/Geissenkl%C3%B6sterle" title="Geissenklösterle">Geissenklösterle</a>, Germany;<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKillin2018"Music_archaeology_of_the_Upper_Palaeolithic"_48-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKillin2018"Music_archaeology_of_the_Upper_Palaeolithic"-48'>[41]</a></sup> <i>bottom</i> a <a href="/wiki/Gudi_(instrument)" title="Gudi (instrument)"><i>gudi</i> bone flute</a> in the modern-day <a href="/wiki/Jiahu" title="Jiahu">Jiahu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wuyang_County" title="Wuyang County">Wuyang</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henan_Province" title="Henan Province">Henan Province</a>.</div></div></div></div>
<p>In the broadest sense, <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_music" title="Prehistoric music">prehistoric music</a>—more commonly termed primitive music in the past<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENettl19561–5_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENettl19561–5-49">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWallinMerkerBrown200021_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWallinMerkerBrown200021-50">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[n 8]</a></sup>—encompasses all music produced in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Literate" title="Literate">preliterate</a> cultures (<a href="/wiki/Prehistory" title="Prehistory">prehistory</a>), beginning at least 6 million years ago when <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">humans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chimpanzee" title="Chimpanzee">chimpanzees</a> <a href="/wiki/Chimpanzee%E2%80%93human_last_common_ancestor" title="Chimpanzee–human last common ancestor">last had a common ancestor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorley20133_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorley20133-52">[44]</a></sup> Music first arose in the <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a> period,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKunejTurk2000235_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKunejTurk2000235-53">[45]</a></sup> though it remains unclear as to whether this was the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Paleolithic" title="Middle Paleolithic">Middle</a> (300,000 to 50,000 <a href="/wiki/Before_Present" title="Before Present">BP</a>) or <a href="/wiki/Upper_Paleolithic" title="Upper Paleolithic">Upper Paleolithic</a> (50,000 to 12,000 BP).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKunejTurk2000235–236_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKunejTurk2000235–236-54">[46]</a></sup> The vast majority of Paleolithic instruments have been found in Europe and date to the Upper Paleolithic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorley201333_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorley201333-55">[47]</a></sup> It is certainly possible that singing emerged far before this time, though this is essentially impossible to confirm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuron200363_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuron200363-56">[48]</a></sup> The potentially oldest instrument is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Divje_Babe_Flute" title="Divje Babe Flute">Divje Babe Flute</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Divje_Babe" title="Divje Babe">Divje Babe</a> cave in Slovenia, dated to 43,000 and 82,000 and made from a young <a href="/wiki/Cave_bear" title="Cave bear">cave bear</a> <a href="/wiki/Femur" title="Femur">femur</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorley201338_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorley201338-57">[49]</a></sup> Purportedly used by <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">Neanderthals</a>, the Divje Babe Flute has received extensive scholarly attention, and whether it is truly a musical instrument or an object formed by animals is the subject of intense debate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorley201338–39_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorley201338–39-47">[40]</a></sup> If the former, it would be the oldest known musical instrument and evidence of a musical culture in the Middle Paleolithic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKunejTurk2000236_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKunejTurk2000236-58">[50]</a></sup> Other than the Divje Babe Flute and three other doubtful flutes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[n 9]</a></sup> there is virtually no surviving Middle Paleolithic musical evidence of any certainty, similar to the <a href="/wiki/Art_of_the_Middle_Paleolithic" title="Art of the Middle Paleolithic">situation in regards to visual art</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKunejTurk2000235_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKunejTurk2000235-53">[45]</a></sup> The earliest objects whose designations as musical instruments are widely accepted are <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic_flutes" title="Paleolithic flutes">bone flutes</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Swabian_Jura" title="Swabian Jura">Swabian Jura</a>, Germany, namely from the <a href="/wiki/Geissenkl%C3%B6sterle" title="Geissenklösterle">Geissenklösterle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hohle_Fels" title="Hohle Fels">Hohle Fels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vogelherd_Cave" title="Vogelherd Cave">Vogelherd</a> caves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorley201342_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorley201342-60">[51]</a></sup> Dated to the <a href="/wiki/Aurignacian" title="Aurignacian">Aurignacian</a> (of the Upper Paleolithic) and used by <a href="/wiki/Early_European_modern_humans" title="Early European modern humans">Early European modern humans</a>, from all three caves there are eight examples, four made from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bird%27s_wing" title="Bird's wing">wing bones</a> of birds and four from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mammoth_ivory" title="Mammoth ivory">mammoth ivory</a>; three of these are near complete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorley201342_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorley201342-60">[51]</a></sup> Three flutes from the Geissenklösterle are dated as the oldest, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 43,150–39,370</span> BP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorley201343_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorley201343-61">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[n 10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Considering the relative complexity of flutes, it is likely earlier instruments existed, akin to objects that are common in later <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hunter_and_gatherer" title="Hunter and gatherer">hunter and gatherer</a> societies, such as <a href="/wiki/Rattle_(percussion_instrument)" title="Rattle (percussion instrument)">rattles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shaker_(musical_instrument)" title="Shaker (musical instrument)">shakers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Drum" title="Drum">drums</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuron200363_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuron200363-56">[48]</a></sup> The absence of  other instruments from and before this time may be due to their use of weaker—and thus more <a href="/wiki/Biodegradation" title="Biodegradation">biodegradable</a>—materials,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKunejTurk2000235_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKunejTurk2000235-53">[45]</a></sup> such as reeds, gourds, skins, and bark.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKillin2018"The_Late_Pleistocene:_Mid/Upper_Palaeolithic_musicality"_63-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKillin2018"The_Late_Pleistocene:_Mid/Upper_Palaeolithic_musicality"-63'>[53]</a></sup> A painting in the <a href="/wiki/Cave_of_the_Trois-Fr%C3%A8res" title="Cave of the Trois-Frères">Cave of the Trois-Frères</a> dating to <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 15,000</span> BCE is thought to depict a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shaman" title="Shaman">shaman</a> playing a <a href="/wiki/Musical_bow" title="Musical bow">musical bow</a>.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTERycroft2001"1._History"_64-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTERycroft2001"1._History"-64'>[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>Prehistoric cultures are thought to have had a wide variety of uses for music, with little unification between different societies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorley2013324_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorley2013324-65">[55]</a></sup> Music was likely in particular value when food and other basic needs were scarce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorley2013324_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorley2013324-65">[55]</a></sup> It is also probable that prehistoric cultures viewed music as intrinsically connected with nature, and may have believed its use influenced the natural world directly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorley2013324_65-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorley2013324-65">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest instruments found in prehistoric China are 12 <a href="/wiki/Gudi_(instrument)" title="Gudi (instrument)"><i>gudi</i> bone flutes</a> in the modern-day <a href="/wiki/Jiahu" title="Jiahu">Jiahu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wuyang_County" title="Wuyang County">Wuyang</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henan_Province" title="Henan Province">Henan Province</a> from <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 6000</span> BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuangsheng200088,_91_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuangsheng200088,_91-67">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[n 11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[n 12]</a></sup> The only instruments dated to the prehistoric <a href="/wiki/Xia_dynasty" title="Xia dynasty">Xia dynasty</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 2070–1600</span>) are two <i><a href="/wiki/Sounding_stone" title="Sounding stone">qing</a></i>, two small bells (one <a href="/wiki/Earthenware" title="Earthenware">earthenware</a>, one <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a>), and a <i><a href="/wiki/Xun_(instrument)" title="Xun (instrument)">xun</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETong1983a23_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETong1983a23-71">[59]</a></sup> Due to this extreme scarcity of surviving instruments and the general uncertainty surrounding most of the Xia, creating a musical narrative of the period is impractical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETong1983a23_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETong1983a23-71">[59]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>, the prehistoric <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_civilisation" title="Indus Valley civilisation">Indus Valley civilisation</a> (from <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 2500–2000</span> BCE in its mature state) has archeological evidence that indicates simple <a href="/wiki/Rattle_(percussion_instrument)" title="Rattle (percussion instrument)">rattles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vessel_flute" title="Vessel flute">vessel flutes</a> were used, while iconographical evidence suggests early harps and drums also existed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlora2000319–320_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlora2000319–320-72">[60]</a></sup> An <a href="/wiki/Ideogram" title="Ideogram">ideogram</a> in the later IVC contains the earliest known depiction of an arched <a href="/wiki/Harp" title="Harp">harp</a>, dated sometime before 1800 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiquity">Antiquity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Antiquity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_music" title="Ancient music">Ancient music</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hurritische_hymne.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="232" data-file-width="475" decoding="async" height="107" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Hurritische_hymne.gif/220px-Hurritische_hymne.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Hurritische_hymne.gif/330px-Hurritische_hymne.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Hurritische_hymne.gif/440px-Hurritische_hymne.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hurritische_hymne.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Drawing of the tablet with the Hymn to <a href="/wiki/Nikkal" title="Nikkal">Nikkal</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1400</span> BCE), the oldest of the <a href="/wiki/Hurrian_songs" title="Hurrian songs">Hurrian songs</a></div></div></div>
<p>Following the <a href="/wiki/History_of_writing" title="History of writing">advent of writing</a>, literate civilizations are termed part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_world" title="Ancient world">ancient world</a>, a periodization which extends from the first <a href="/wiki/Sumerian_literature" title="Sumerian literature">Sumerian literature</a> of <a href="/wiki/Abu_Salabikh" title="Abu Salabikh">Abu Salabikh</a> (now Southern Iraq) of <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 2600</span> BCE, until the <a href="/wiki/Post-classical_history" title="Post-classical history">Post-classical era</a> of the 6th century CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimbl2000216_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimbl2000216-74">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[63]</a></sup> Though the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_music" title="Ancient music">music of Ancient societies</a> was extremely diverse, some fundamental concepts arise prominently in virtually all of them, namely <a href="/wiki/Monophony" title="Monophony">monophony</a>, <a href="/wiki/Improvisation" title="Improvisation">improvisation</a> and the dominance of text in musical settings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrout19734–5,_11_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrout19734–5,_11-76">[64]</a></sup> Varying <a href="/wiki/Song" title="Song">song</a> forms were present in Ancient cultures, including China, Egypt, Greece, India, Mesopotamia, Rome and the Middle East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChew2001_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChew2001-77">[65]</a></sup> The text, rhythm and melodies of these songs were closely aligned, as was music in general, with magic, science and religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChew2001_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChew2001-77">[65]</a></sup> Complex song forms developed in later ancient societies, particularly the national festivals of China, Greece and India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChew2001_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChew2001-77">[65]</a></sup> Later Ancient societies also saw increased trade and transmission of musical ideas and instruments, often shepherded by the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a>.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2009"Migration_and_Cultural_Exchange"_78-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2009"Migration_and_Cultural_Exchange"-78'>[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELam2001b_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELam2001b-79">[67]</a></sup> For example, a tuning key for a <i>qin</i>-zither from 4th–5th centuries BCE China includes considerable Persian iconography.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTELawergren2001"4._1st_millennium_BCE:_(iii)_Achaemenid_period,_550–331_BCE"_80-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawergren2001"4._1st_millennium_BCE:_(iii)_Achaemenid_period,_550–331_BCE"-80'>[68]</a></sup> In general, not enough information exists to make many other generalizations about ancient music between cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChew2001_77-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChew2001-77">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>The few actual examples of ancient music notation that survive usually exist on <a href="/wiki/Papyrus" title="Papyrus">papyrus</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clay_tablets" title="Clay tablets">clay tablets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChew2001_77-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChew2001-77">[65]</a></sup> Information on musical practices, genres, and thought is mainly available through literature, visual depictions, and increasingly as the period progresses, instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChew2001_77-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChew2001-77">[65]</a></sup> The oldest surviving written music is the <a href="/wiki/Hurrian_songs" title="Hurrian songs">Hurrian songs</a> from <a href="/wiki/Ugarit" title="Ugarit">Ugarit</a>, Syria. Of these, the oldest is the Hymn to <a href="/wiki/Nikkal" title="Nikkal">Nikkal</a> (hymn no. 6; h. 6), which is somewhat complete and dated to <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1400</span> BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKilmer197469–70_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKilmer197469–70-81">[69]</a></sup> However, the <a href="/wiki/Seikilos_epitaph" title="Seikilos epitaph">Seikilos epitaph</a> is the earliest entirely complete noted musical composition. Dated to the 2nd-Century CE or later, it is an <a href="/wiki/Epitaph" title="Epitaph">epitaph</a>, perhaps for the wife of the unknown Seikilos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERandel2003361,_767_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandel2003361,_767-82">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Shang_and_Zhou">Shang and Zhou</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Shang and Zhou">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1062260506" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25em; ; background-color: #c6dbf7;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>They strike the bells, <i>kin</i>, <i>kin</i>,<br/>They play the <i>se</i>-zither, play the <i>qin</i>-zither,<br/>The mouth organ and chime stones sound together;<br/>They sing the <i>Ya</i> and <i>Nan</i> Odes,<br/>And perform flawlessly upon their flutes.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style=""><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shijing" title="Shijing">Shijing</a></i>, Ode 208, <i>Gu Zhong</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESoMajor200019_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESoMajor200019-83">[71]</a></sup><br/>Translated by John S. Major<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESoMajor200033_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESoMajor200033-84">[72]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>By the mid-13th century BCE, the late <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a> (1600–1046 BCE) had developed writing, which mostly exists as divinatory <a href="/wiki/Oracle_bone_script" title="Oracle bone script">inscriptions</a> on the ritualistic <a href="/wiki/Oracle_bone" title="Oracle bone">oracle bones</a> but also as <a href="/wiki/Chinese_bronze_inscriptions" title="Chinese bronze inscriptions">bronze inscriptions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[74]</a></sup> As many as 11 oracle script characters may refer to music to some extent, some of which could be iconographical representations of instruments themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETong1983a64_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETong1983a64-87">[75]</a></sup> The stone bells <i>qing</i> appears to have been particularly popular with the Shang ruling class,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[n 13]</a></sup> and while no surviving flutes have been dated to the Shang,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETong1983b167_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETong1983b167-90">[77]</a></sup> oracle script evidence suggests they used <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ocarinas" title="Ocarinas">ocarinas</a> (<i>xun</i>), <a href="/wiki/Transverse_flute" title="Transverse flute">transverse flute</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Xiao_(flute)" title="Xiao (flute)">xiao</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Dizi_(instrument)" title="Dizi (instrument)">dizi</a></i>), douple pipes, the mouthorgan (<i><a href="/wiki/Sheng_(instrument)" title="Sheng (instrument)">sheng</a></i>), and maybe the <a href="/wiki/Pan_flute" title="Pan flute">pan flute</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Paixiao" title="Paixiao">paixiao</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETong1983b182_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETong1983b182-91">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[n 14]</a></sup> Due to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_China" title="Bronze Age China">advent of the bronze in 2000 BCE</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESoMajor200013_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESoMajor200013-94">[80]</a></sup> the Shang used the material for bells—the <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=%E9%88%B4&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="鈴 (page does not exist)">ling</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E9%88%B4" title="zh:鈴">zh</a>]</span></i> (鈴), <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=%E9%90%83&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="鐃 (page does not exist)">nao</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E9%90%83" title="zh:鐃">zh</a>]</span></i> (鐃) and <i>zhong</i> (鐘)<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2009"Archaeological_Evidence_of_Musical_Instruments"_68-1'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2009"Archaeological_Evidence_of_Musical_Instruments"-68'>[58]</a></sup>—that can be differentiated in two ways: those with or without a clapper and those struck on the inside or outside.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETong1983b145_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETong1983b145-95">[81]</a></sup> Drums, which are not found from before the Shang,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETong1983a119_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETong1983a119-96">[82]</a></sup> sometimes used bronze, though they were more often wooden (<i><a href="/wiki/Bangu_(drum)" title="Bangu (drum)">bangu</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETong1983a152_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETong1983a152-97">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2009"Archaeological_Evidence_of_Musical_Instruments"_68-2'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2009"Archaeological_Evidence_of_Musical_Instruments"-68'>[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[n 15]</a></sup> The aforementioned wind instruments certainly existed by the <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou dynasty</a> (1046–256 BCE), as did the first Chinese string instruments: the <i><a href="/wiki/Guqin" title="Guqin">qin</a></i> (or <i>guqin</i>) and <i><a href="/wiki/Se_(instrument)" title="Se (instrument)">se</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Zither" title="Zither">zithers</a>.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2009"Archaeological_Evidence_of_Musical_Instruments"_68-3'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2009"Archaeological_Evidence_of_Musical_Instruments"-68'>[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[n 16]</a></sup> The Zhou saw the emergence of major court ensembles and the well known <a href="/wiki/Tomb_of_Marquis_Yi_of_Zeng" title="Tomb of Marquis Yi of Zeng">Tomb of Marquis Yi of Zeng</a> (after 433 BCE) contains a variety of complex and decorated instruments.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2009"Archaeological_Evidence_of_Musical_Instruments"_68-4'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2009"Archaeological_Evidence_of_Musical_Instruments"-68'>[58]</a></sup> Of the tomb, the by-far most notable instrument is the monumental <a href="/wiki/Bianzhong_of_Marquis_Yi_of_Zeng" title="Bianzhong of Marquis Yi of Zeng">set of 65 tuned <i>bianzhong</i> bells</a>, which range five octaves requiring at least five players; they are still playable and include rare inscriptions on music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBagley200036–37_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBagley200036–37-102">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E6%B9%96%E5%8C%97%E5%8D%9A%E7%89%A9%E9%A4%A8%E6%9B%BE%E4%BE%AF%E4%B9%99%E7%B7%A8%E9%90%98.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3024" data-file-width="4032" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/%E6%B9%96%E5%8C%97%E5%8D%9A%E7%89%A9%E9%A4%A8%E6%9B%BE%E4%BE%AF%E4%B9%99%E7%B7%A8%E9%90%98.jpg/220px-%E6%B9%96%E5%8C%97%E5%8D%9A%E7%89%A9%E9%A4%A8%E6%9B%BE%E4%BE%AF%E4%B9%99%E7%B7%A8%E9%90%98.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/%E6%B9%96%E5%8C%97%E5%8D%9A%E7%89%A9%E9%A4%A8%E6%9B%BE%E4%BE%AF%E4%B9%99%E7%B7%A8%E9%90%98.jpg/330px-%E6%B9%96%E5%8C%97%E5%8D%9A%E7%89%A9%E9%A4%A8%E6%9B%BE%E4%BE%AF%E4%B9%99%E7%B7%A8%E9%90%98.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/%E6%B9%96%E5%8C%97%E5%8D%9A%E7%89%A9%E9%A4%A8%E6%9B%BE%E4%BE%AF%E4%B9%99%E7%B7%A8%E9%90%98.jpg/440px-%E6%B9%96%E5%8C%97%E5%8D%9A%E7%89%A9%E9%A4%A8%E6%9B%BE%E4%BE%AF%E4%B9%99%E7%B7%A8%E9%90%98.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E6%B9%96%E5%8C%97%E5%8D%9A%E7%89%A9%E9%A4%A8%E6%9B%BE%E4%BE%AF%E4%B9%99%E7%B7%A8%E9%90%98.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The monumental <a href="/wiki/Bianzhong_of_Marquis_Yi_of_Zeng" title="Bianzhong of Marquis Yi of Zeng">Bianzhong of Marquis Yi of Zeng</a>, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 5th century</span> BCE, from <a href="/wiki/Hubei" title="Hubei">Hubei</a></div></div></div>
<p>Ancient Chinese instruments served both practical and ceremonial means. People used them to appeal to <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a> forces for survival needs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELam2001a_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELam2001a-103">[87]</a></sup> while pan flutes may have been used to attract birds while hunting,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuangsheng200094_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuangsheng200094-104">[88]</a></sup> and drums were common in sacrifices and military ceremonies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETong1983a119_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETong1983a119-96">[82]</a></sup> Chinese music has always been closely associated with dance, literature and fine arts;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERandel2003[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid02rFSecPhEsCpgPA260_260]_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandel2003[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid02rFSecPhEsCpgPA260_260]-105">[89]</a></sup> many early Chinese thinkers also equated music with proper morality and governance of society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESoMajor200023_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESoMajor200023-106">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[n 17]</a></sup> Throughout the Shang and Zhou music was a symbol of power for the Imperial court,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuangsheng200089_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuangsheng200089-110">[93]</a></sup> being utilized in religious services as well as the <a href="/wiki/Ancestor_veneration_in_China" title="Ancestor veneration in China">celebration of ancestors</a> and heroes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELam2001a_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELam2001a-103">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrindley201226_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrindley201226-111">[94]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 551–479</span>) formally designated the music concerned with ritual and ideal morality as the superior <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Yayue" title="Yayue">yayue</a></i></span> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">雅樂</span></span>; "proper music"), in opposition to <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn"><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Suyue&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Suyue (page does not exist)">suyue</a></i></span> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">俗樂</span></span>; "vernacular/popular music"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELam2001a_103-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELam2001a-103">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESoMajor200025_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESoMajor200025-112">[95]</a></sup> which included virtually all non-ceremonial music, but particularly any that was considered excessive or lascivious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESoMajor200025_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESoMajor200025-112">[95]</a></sup> During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warring_States_Period" title="Warring States Period">Warring States Period</a> when of Confucius's lifetime, officials often ignored this distinction, preferring more lively <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">suyue</i></span> music and using the older <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">yayue</i></span> traditional solely for political means.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESo200025_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESo200025-113">[96]</a></sup> Confucius and disciples such as <a href="/wiki/Mencius" title="Mencius">Mencius</a> considered this preference virtueless and saw ill of the leaders' ignorance of <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Ganying" title="Ganying">ganying</a></i></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESoMajor200023,_29_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESoMajor200023,_29-114">[97]</a></sup> a theory that held music was intrinsically connected to the universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrindley20123–5_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrindley20123–5-115">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESoMajor200029_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESoMajor200029-116">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[n 18]</a></sup> Thus, many aspects of Ancient Chinese music were aligned with cosmology: the 12 pitch <a href="/wiki/Sh%C3%AD-%C3%A8r-l%C7%9C" title="Shí-èr-lǜ">shí-èr-lǜ</a> system corresponded equally with certain weights and measurements; the <a href="/wiki/Pentatonic_scale" title="Pentatonic scale">pentatonic scale</a> with the five <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Wuxing_(Chinese_philosophy)" title="Wuxing (Chinese philosophy)">wuxing</a></i></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESoMajor200029_116-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESoMajor200029-116">[99]</a></sup> and the eight tone classification of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Chinese_instruments" title="List of Chinese instruments">Chinese instruments</a> of <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">bayin</i></span> with the eight symbols of <i><a href="/wiki/Bagua" title="Bagua">bagua</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2009"Classification_and_Context_of_Musical_Instruments"_118-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2009"Classification_and_Context_of_Musical_Instruments"-118'>[100]</a></sup> No actual music or texts on the performance practices of Ancient Chinese musicians survive.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalm2019"Han_dynasty_(3rd_century_BCE–3rd_century_CE):_musical_events_and_foreign_influences"_119-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalm2019"Han_dynasty_(3rd_century_BCE–3rd_century_CE):_musical_events_and_foreign_influences"-119'>[101]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_Classics" title="Five Classics">Five Classics</a> of the Zhou dynasty include musical commentary; the <i><a href="/wiki/I_Ching" title="I Ching">I Ching</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Spring_and_Autumn_Annals" title="Spring and Autumn Annals"><i>Chunqiu</i> Spring and Autumn Annals</a> make references, while the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liji" title="Liji"><i>Liji</i> Book of Rites</a> contains a substantial discussion (see the chapter <a href="/wiki/Record_of_Music" title="Record of Music"><i>Yue Ji</i> Record of Music</a>).<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalm2019"Ancient_artifacts_and_writings"_45-2'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalm2019"Ancient_artifacts_and_writings"-45'>[39]</a></sup> While the <a href="/wiki/Classic_of_Music" title="Classic of Music"><i>Yue Jing</i> Classic of Music</a> is lost,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESoMajor200013_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESoMajor200013-94">[80]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shijing" title="Shijing"><i>Shijing</i> Classic of Poetry</a> contains 160 texts to now lost songs from the <a href="/wiki/Western_Zhou" title="Western Zhou">Western Zhou</a> period (1045–771).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFalkenhausen2000102_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFalkenhausen2000102-120">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Qin_and_Han">Qin and Han</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Qin and Han">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shanghai_Museum_2006_17-15.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Shanghai_Museum_2006_17-15.jpg/220px-Shanghai_Museum_2006_17-15.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Shanghai_Museum_2006_17-15.jpg/330px-Shanghai_Museum_2006_17-15.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Shanghai_Museum_2006_17-15.jpg/440px-Shanghai_Museum_2006_17-15.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shanghai_Museum_2006_17-15.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two musicians of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Han_Dynasty" title="Eastern Han Dynasty">Eastern Han Dynasty</a> (25–220 CE), <a href="/wiki/Shanghai_Museum" title="Shanghai Museum">Shanghai Museum</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin dynasty</a> (221–206 BCE), established by <a href="/wiki/Qin_Shi_Huang" title="Qin Shi Huang">Qin Shi Huang</a>, lasted for only 15 years, but the purported <a href="/wiki/Burning_of_books_and_burying_of_scholars" title="Burning of books and burying of scholars">burning of books</a> resulted in a substantial loss of previous musical literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELam2001b_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELam2001b-79">[67]</a></sup> The Qin saw the <i><a href="/wiki/Guzheng" title="Guzheng">guzheng</a></i> become a particularly popular instrument; as a more portable and louder zither, it meet the needs of an emerging popular music scene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELawergren200083_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawergren200083-121">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[n 19]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> (202 BCE – 220 CE), there were attempts to reconstruct the music of the Shang and Zhou, as it was now "idealized as perfect".<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2009"Classification_and_Context_of_Musical_Instruments"_118-1'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2009"Classification_and_Context_of_Musical_Instruments"-118'>[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELam2001b_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELam2001b-79">[67]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Music_Bureau" title="Music Bureau">Music Bureau</a>, the <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">Yuefu</i></span>, was founded or at its height by at least 120 BCE under <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Wu_of_Han" title="Emperor Wu of Han">Emperor Wu of Han</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBirrell1993xix_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBirrell1993xix-123">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[n 20]</a></sup> and was responsible for collecting folksongs. The purpose of this was twofold; it allowed the Imperial Court to properly understand the thoughts of the common people,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERandel2003[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid02rFSecPhEsCpgPA260_260]_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandel2003[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid02rFSecPhEsCpgPA260_260]-105">[89]</a></sup> and it was also an opportunity for the Imperial Court to adapt and manipulate the songs to suit propaganda and political purposes.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2009"Classification_and_Context_of_Musical_Instruments"_118-2'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2009"Classification_and_Context_of_Musical_Instruments"-118'>[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELam2001b_79-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELam2001b-79">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[n 21]</a></sup> Employing ceremonial, entertainment-oriented and military musicians,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFurniss200923_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFurniss200923-127">[106]</a></sup> the Bureau also performed at a variety of venues, wrote new music, and set music to commissioned poetry by noted figures such as <a href="/wiki/Sima_Xiangru" title="Sima Xiangru">Sima Xiangru</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBirrell1993xx_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBirrell1993xx-128">[107]</a></sup> The Han dynasty had officially adopted <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a> as the state philosophy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELam2001b_79-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELam2001b-79">[67]</a></sup> and the <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">ganying</i></span> theories became a dominant philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrindley2012192_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrindley2012192-129">[108]</a></sup> In practice, however, many officials ignored or downplayed Confucius's high regard for <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">yayue</i></span> over <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">suyue</i></span> music, preferring to engage in the more lively and informal later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFurniss200924_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFurniss200924-130">[109]</a></sup> By 7 BCE the Bureau employed 829 musicians; that year <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Ai_of_Han" title="Emperor Ai of Han">Emperor Ai</a> either disbanded or downsized the department,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELam2001b_79-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELam2001b-79">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBirrell1993xx_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBirrell1993xx-128">[107]</a></sup> due to financial limitations,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELam2001b_79-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELam2001b-79">[67]</a></sup> and the Bureau's increasingly prominent <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">suyue</i></span> music which conflicted with Confucianism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFurniss200923_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFurniss200923-127">[106]</a></sup> The Han dynasty saw a preponderance of foreign musical influences from the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> and <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>: the emerging Silk Road led to the exchange of musical instruments,<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2009"Migration_and_Cultural_Exchange"_78-1'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2009"Migration_and_Cultural_Exchange"-78'>[66]</a></sup> and allowed travelers such as <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Qian" title="Zhang Qian">Zhang Qian</a> to relay with new musical genres and techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELam2001b_79-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELam2001b-79">[67]</a></sup> Instruments from said cultural transmission include metal trumpets and instruments similar to the modern <a href="/wiki/Oboe" title="Oboe">oboe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oud" title="Oud">oud</a> lute, the latter which became the <a href="/wiki/Pipa" title="Pipa">pipa</a>.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2009"Migration_and_Cultural_Exchange"_78-2'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2009"Migration_and_Cultural_Exchange"-78'>[66]</a></sup> Other preexisting instruments greatly increased in popularity, such as the <i>qing</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuangsheng200094_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuangsheng200094-104">[88]</a></sup> panpipes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuangsheng200094–95_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuangsheng200094–95-131">[110]</a></sup> and particularly the <i>qin</i>-zither (or <i>guqin</i>), which was from then on the most revered instrument, associated with good character and morality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MET2_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-MET2-100">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greece">Greece</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Greece">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_ancient_Greece" title="Music of ancient Greece">Music of ancient Greece</a></div>
<p>Greek written history extends far back into <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greece</a>, and was a major part of ancient <a href="/wiki/Theatre_of_ancient_Greece" title="Theatre of ancient Greece">Greek theatre</a>. In ancient Greece, mixed-gender choruses performed for entertainment, celebration and spiritual reasons.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Instruments included the most important wind instrument, the double-reed <a href="/wiki/Aulos" title="Aulos">aulos</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMathiesen1999177_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMathiesen1999177-132">[111]</a></sup> as well as the plucked <a href="/wiki/String_instrument" title="String instrument">string instrument</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Lyre" title="Lyre">lyre</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrout197324_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrout197324-133">[112]</a></sup> especially the special kind called a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kithara" title="Kithara">kithara</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_in_ancient_India" title="Music in ancient India">Music in ancient India</a></div>
<p>The principal sources on the <a href="/wiki/Music_in_ancient_India" title="Music in ancient India">music of ancient India</a> are textual and iconographical; specifically, some theoretical treatises in <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> survive, there are brief mentions in general literature and many sculptures of Ancient Indian musicians and their instruments exist.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPowersKatz2020"1._Introduction"_134-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPowersKatz2020"1._Introduction"-134'>[113]</a></sup> Ancient <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit_literature" title="Sanskrit literature">Sanskrit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pali_literature" title="Pali literature">Pali</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prakrit_literature" title="Prakrit literature">Prakrit literature</a> frequently contains musical references, from the <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a> to the works of <a href="/wiki/Kalidasa" title="Kalidasa">Kalidasa</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ilango_Adigal" title="Ilango Adigal">Ilango Adigal</a>'s epic <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silappatikaram" title="Silappatikaram">Silappatikaram</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPowersKatz2020"2._To_the_mid-16th_century:_(i)_The_ancient_period,_(c)_References_in_general_literature"_135-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPowersKatz2020"2._To_the_mid-16th_century:_(i)_The_ancient_period,_(c)_References_in_general_literature"-135'>[114]</a></sup> Despite this, little is known on the actual musical practices of ancient India and the information available forces a somewhat homogeneous perspective on the music of the time, even though evidence indicates that in reality, it was far more diverse.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPowersKatz2020"1._Introduction"_134-1'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPowersKatz2020"1._Introduction"-134'>[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>The monumental arts treatise <i><a href="/wiki/Natya_Shastra" title="Natya Shastra">Natya Shastra</a></i> is among the earliest and chief sources for Ancient Indian music; the music portions alone are likely from the <a href="/wiki/Gupta_Empire" title="Gupta Empire">Gupta period</a> (4th century to 6th century CE).<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPowersKatz2020"2._To_the_mid-16th_century:_(i)_The_ancient_period,_(a)_Treatises"_136-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPowersKatz2020"2._To_the_mid-16th_century:_(i)_The_ancient_period,_(a)_Treatises"-136'>[115]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Persia_and_Mesopotamia">Persia and Mesopotamia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Persia and Mesopotamia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Mesopotamia" title="Music of Mesopotamia">Music of Mesopotamia</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Up_to_the_Achaemenid_period">Up to the Achaemenid period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Up to the Achaemenid period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bull_Headed_Lyre_of_Ur.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3180" data-file-width="2385" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Bull_Headed_Lyre_of_Ur.jpg/220px-Bull_Headed_Lyre_of_Ur.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Bull_Headed_Lyre_of_Ur.jpg/330px-Bull_Headed_Lyre_of_Ur.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Bull_Headed_Lyre_of_Ur.jpg/440px-Bull_Headed_Lyre_of_Ur.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bull_Headed_Lyre_of_Ur.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Bull_Headed_Lyre_of_Ur" title="Bull Headed Lyre of Ur">Bull Headed Lyre of Ur</a>, found in the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Cemetery_at_Ur" title="Royal Cemetery at Ur">Royal Cemetery at Ur</a>, is the best known of the ancient <a href="/wiki/Lyres_of_Ur" title="Lyres of Ur">Lyres of Ur</a></div></div></div>
<p>In general, it is impossible to create a thorough outline of the earliest music in Persia due to a paucity of surviving records.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuring1991a39_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuring1991a39-137">[116]</a></sup> Evidenced by <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 3300–3100</span> BCE <a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">Elam</a> depictions, arched harps are the first affirmation of Persian music, though it is probable that they existed well before their artistic depictions.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTELawergren2001"2._3rd_millennium_BCE:_(i)_Arched_harps"_138-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawergren2001"2._3rd_millennium_BCE:_(i)_Arched_harps"-138'>[117]</a></sup> Elamite bull lyres from <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 2450</span> have been found in <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a>, while more than 40 small Oxus <a href="/wiki/Trumpet" title="Trumpet">trumpets</a> have been found in <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Margiana" title="Margiana">Margiana</a>, dated to the <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 2200–1750</span> <a href="/wiki/Bactria%E2%80%93Margiana_Archaeological_Complex" title="Bactria–Margiana Archaeological Complex">Bactria–Margiana Archaeological Complex</a>.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTELawergren2001"2._3rd_millennium_BCE:_(ii)_Bull_lyres,_(iii)_Trumpets"_139-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawergren2001"2._3rd_millennium_BCE:_(ii)_Bull_lyres,_(iii)_Trumpets"-139'>[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[n 22]</a></sup> The oxus trumpets seem to have had a close association with both religion and animals; a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zoroastrian" title="Zoroastrian">Zoroastrian</a> myth in which Jamshid attract animals with the trumpet suggests that the Elamites used them for hunting.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTELawergren2001"2._3rd_millennium_BCE:_(iii)_Trumpets"_140-1'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawergren2001"2._3rd_millennium_BCE:_(iii)_Trumpets"-140'>[119]</a></sup> In many ways the earliest known musical cultures of Iran are strongly connected with those of Mesopotamia. Ancient arched harps (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 3000</span>) also exist in the latter and the scarcity of instruments makes it unclear as to which culture the harp originated.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTELawergren2001"2._3rd_millennium_BCE:_(i)_Arched_harps"_138-1'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawergren2001"2._3rd_millennium_BCE:_(i)_Arched_harps"-138'>[117]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lyres_of_Ur" title="Lyres of Ur">Far more bull lyres</a> survive in <a href="/wiki/Ur" title="Ur">Ur</a> of Mesopotamia, notably the <a href="/wiki/Bull_Headed_Lyre_of_Ur" title="Bull Headed Lyre of Ur">Bull Headed Lyre of Ur</a>, though they are nearly identical to their contemporary Elamite counterparts.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTELawergren2009"i._Third_Millennium_B.C.E.:_(2)_Bull_lyres_in_western_Iran"_142-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawergren2009"i._Third_Millennium_B.C.E.:_(2)_Bull_lyres_in_western_Iran"-142'>[120]</a></sup> From the evidence in terracotta plaques, by the 2nd-century BCE the arched harp was displaced by the angular harps, which existed in 20-string vertical and nine-string horizontal variants.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTELawergren2001"3._2nd_millennium_BCE:_(i)_Angular_harps"_143-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawergren2001"3._2nd_millennium_BCE:_(i)_Angular_harps"-143'>[121]</a></sup> Lutes were
