purportedly used in Mesopotamia by at least 2300 BCE, but not until <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1300</span> BCE do they appear in Iran, where they became the dominant string instruments of <a href="/wiki/Western_Iran" title="Western Iran">Western Iran</a>, though the available evidence suggests its popularity was outside of the elite.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTELawergren2001"3._2nd_millennium_BCE:_(ii)_Lutes"_144-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawergren2001"3._2nd_millennium_BCE:_(ii)_Lutes"-144'>[122]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Rock_relief" title="Rock relief">rock reliefs</a> of <a href="/wiki/Kul-e_Farah" title="Kul-e Farah">Kul-e Farah</a> show that sophisticated Persian court ensembles emerged in the 1st-century BCE, in the which the central instrument was the arched harp.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTELawergren2001"4._1st_millennium_BCE:_(i)_Elamite_harp_ensembles"_145-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawergren2001"4._1st_millennium_BCE:_(i)_Elamite_harp_ensembles"-145'>[123]</a></sup> The prominence of musicians in these certain rock reliefs suggests they were essential in religious ceremonies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaele198934–35_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaele198934–35-146">[124]</a></sup>
</p><p>Like earlier periods, extremely little contemporary information on the music of the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a> (550–330 BCE) exists.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTELawergren2001"4._1st_millennium_BCE:_(iii)_Achaemenid_period,_550–331_BCE"_80-1'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawergren2001"4._1st_millennium_BCE:_(iii)_Achaemenid_period,_550–331_BCE"-80'>[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFarhat20043_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFarhat20043-147">[125]</a></sup> Most knowledge on the Achaemenid musical culture comes from Greek historians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFarhat20043_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFarhat20043-147">[125]</a></sup> In his <i><a href="/wiki/Histories_(Herodotus)" title="Histories (Herodotus)">Histories</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> noted that Achamenid priests did not use <a href="/wiki/Aulos" title="Aulos">aulos</a> music in their ceremonies, while <a href="/wiki/Xenophon" title="Xenophon">Xenophon</a> reflected on his visit to Persia in the <i><a href="/wiki/Cyropaedia" title="Cyropaedia">Cyropaedia</a></i>, mentioning the presence of many female singers at court.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTELawergren2001"4._1st_millennium_BCE:_(iii)_Achaemenid_period,_550–331_BCE"_80-2'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawergren2001"4._1st_millennium_BCE:_(iii)_Achaemenid_period,_550–331_BCE"-80'>[68]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Athenaeus" title="Athenaeus">Athenaeus</a> also mentions female singers when noting that 329 of them had been taken from the <a href="/wiki/King_of_Kings" title="King of Kings">King of Kings</a> <a href="/wiki/Darius_III" title="Darius III">Darius III</a> by Macedonian general <a href="/wiki/Parmenion" title="Parmenion">Parmenion</a>.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTELawergren2001"4._1st_millennium_BCE:_(iii)_Achaemenid_period,_550–331_BCE"_80-3'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawergren2001"4._1st_millennium_BCE:_(iii)_Achaemenid_period,_550–331_BCE"-80'>[68]</a></sup> Later Persian texts assert that <i><a href="/wiki/Gusans" title="Gusans">gōsān</a></i> poet-musician <a href="/wiki/Minstrel" title="Minstrel">minstrels</a> were prominent and of considerable status in court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoyce195720–21_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyce195720–21-148">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Parthian_and_Sasanian_periods">Parthian and Sasanian periods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Parthian and Sasanian periods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Parthian_music" title="Parthian music">Parthian music</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_music" title="Sasanian music">Sasanian music</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Parthian_lute_player.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="410" decoding="async" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Parthian_lute_player.jpg/130px-Parthian_lute_player.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Parthian_lute_player.jpg/195px-Parthian_lute_player.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Parthian_lute_player.jpg/260px-Parthian_lute_player.jpg 2x" width="130"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Parthian_lute_player.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Terracotta statue of a Parthian lute player</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian Empire</a> (247 BCE to 224 CE) saw an increase in textual and iconographical depictions of musical activity and instruments. 2nd century BCE Parthian <i><a href="/wiki/Rhyton" title="Rhyton">rhuta</a></i> (drinking horns) found in the ancient capital of <a href="/wiki/Nisa,_Turkmenistan" title="Nisa, Turkmenistan">Nisa</a> include some of the most vivid depictions of musicians from the time. Pictorial evidence such as <a href="/wiki/Terracotta" title="Terracotta">terracotta</a> plaques show female harpists, while plaques from <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a> show <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panpipes" title="Panpipes">panpipes</a>, as well as string (harps, lutes and lyres) and percussion instruments (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tambourines" title="Tambourines">tambourines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Clapper_(musical_instrument)" title="Clapper (musical instrument)">clappers</a>). Bronze statues from <a href="/wiki/Dura-Europos" title="Dura-Europos">Dura-Europos</a> depict larger panpipes and double aulos. Music was evidently used in ceremonies and celebrations; a Parthian-era stone <a href="/wiki/Frieze" title="Frieze">frieze</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hatra" title="Hatra">Hatra</a> shows a wedding where musicians are included, playing trumpets, tambourines, and a variety of flutes. Other textual and iconographical evidence indicates the continued prominence of <i>gōsān</i> minstrels. However, like the Achaemenid period, Greek writers continue to be a major source for information on Parthian music. <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a> recorded that the <i>gōsān</i> learned songs telling tales of gods and noblemen, while <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a> similarly records the <i>gōsān</i> lauding Parthian heroes and mocking Roman ones. Plutarch also records, much to his bafflement, that <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhoptra" title="Rhoptra">rhoptra</a></i> (large drums) were used by the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_army" title="Parthian army">Parthian army</a> to prepare for war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELawergren2001a_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawergren2001a-149">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Harp-Sassanid.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="469" data-file-width="650" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Harp-Sassanid.png/220px-Harp-Sassanid.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Harp-Sassanid.png/330px-Harp-Sassanid.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Harp-Sassanid.png/440px-Harp-Sassanid.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Harp-Sassanid.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 379 CE</span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bas_relief" title="Bas relief">Bas relief</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sassanid" title="Sassanid">Sassanid</a> women playing the <i><a href="/wiki/Chang_(instrument)" title="Chang (instrument)">chang</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Taq-e_Bostan" title="Taq-e Bostan">Taq-e Bostan</a>, Iran</div></div></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sasanian" title="Sasanian">Sasanian</a> period (226–651 CE), however, has left ample evidence of music. This influx of Sasanian records suggests a prominent musical culture in the Empire,<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFarhat2012"Historic_Retrospective"_40-1'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFarhat2012"Historic_Retrospective"-40'>[35]</a></sup> especially in the areas dominated by <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a>.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTELawergren2001"5._Sassanian_period,_224–651_CE."_150-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawergren2001"5._Sassanian_period,_224–651_CE."-150'>[128]</a></sup> Many Sassanian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shahanshah" title="Shahanshah">Shahanshahs</a> were ardent supporters of music, including the founder of the empire <a href="/wiki/Ardashir_I" title="Ardashir I">Ardashir I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bahram_V" title="Bahram V">Bahram V</a>.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTELawergren2001"5._Sassanian_period,_224–651_CE."_150-1'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawergren2001"5._Sassanian_period,_224–651_CE."-150'>[128]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Khosrow_II" title="Khosrow II">Khosrow II</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><abbr title="reigned">r.</abbr> 590–628</span>) was the most outstanding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patron" title="Patron">patron</a>, his reign being regarded as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Golden_age" title="Golden age">golden age</a> of Persian music.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTELawergren2001"5._Sassanian_period,_224–651_CE."_150-2'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawergren2001"5._Sassanian_period,_224–651_CE."-150'>[128]</a></sup> Musicians in Khosrow's service include Āzādvar-e Changi,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[n 23]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bamshad" title="Bamshad">Bamshad</a>, the harpist <a href="/wiki/Nagisa_(harpist)" title="Nagisa (harpist)">Nagisa</a> (Nakisa), <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ramtin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ramtin (page does not exist)">Ramtin</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sarkash" title="Sarkash">Sarkash</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barbad" title="Barbad">Barbad</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuring1991a39_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuring1991a39-137">[116]</a></sup> who was the most famous.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFarhat2001"1._History."_152-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFarhat2001"1._History."-152'>[129]</a></sup> These musicians were usually active as <a href="/wiki/Minstrel" title="Minstrel">minstrels</a>, which were performers who worked as both court poets and musicians;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECurtis2003138_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECurtis2003138-153">[130]</a></sup> in the Sassanian Empire there was little distinction between poetry and music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuring1991b154_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuring1991b154-154">[131]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_Arab_and_African_cultures">Other Arab and African cultures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Other Arab and African cultures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Western African <a href="/wiki/Nok_culture" title="Nok culture">Nok culture</a> (modern-day Nigeria) existed from <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 500–200</span> BCE and left a considerable amount of sculptures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[132]</a></sup> Among these are depictions of music, such as a man who shakes two objects thought to be <a href="/wiki/Maraca" title="Maraca">maracas</a>. Another sculpture includes a man with his mouth opening (possibly singing) while there is also a sculpture of a man playing a drum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-classical_era">Post-classical era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Post-classical era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Japanese_gagaku_music">Japanese <i>gagaku</i> music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Japanese gagaku music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gagaku" title="Gagaku">Gagaku</a></div>
<p>The imperial court of Japan developed <i><a href="/wiki/Gagaku" title="Gagaku">gagaku</a></i> ((<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">雅楽</span></span>); <abbr title="literal translation">lit.</abbr><span style="white-space: nowrap;"> </span>'elegant music') music, originating from the <i>Gagakuryō</i> imperial music academy established in 701 CE during the <a href="/wiki/Asuka_period" title="Asuka period">Asuka period</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201636_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201636-157">[134]</a></sup> Though the word <i>gagaku</i> derives from the Chinese <i>yayue</i> music, the latter originally referred to Confucian ritual music, while <i>gagaku</i> extends to many genres, styles and instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201636_157-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201636-157">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarett2001"1._History"_158-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarett2001"1._History"-158'>[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[n 24]</a></sup> In the tradition's early history, the three main genres were <i>wagaku</i> (native Japanese music), <i>sankangaku</i> (music from the <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms_of_Korea" title="Three Kingdoms of Korea">Three Kingdoms of Korea</a>) and <i>tōgaku</i> (music from China's <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a>), as well as more minor genres such as <i>toragaku</i>, <i>gigaku</i>, and <i>rin’yūgaku</i>. Uniquely among Asian music of this time, there are numerous extant scores of <i>gagaku</i> music from the 8th to 11th centuries.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarett2001"1._History"_158-2'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarett2001"1._History"-158'>[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>A major shift in <i>gagaku</i> music occurred in the 9th century, namely the development of a distinction between <i><a href="/wiki/T%C5%8Dgaku" title="Tōgaku">tōgaku</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Komagaku" title="Komagaku">komagaku</a></i> music. <i>Tōgaku</i> was a Chinese-influenced style, which combined with the <i>rin’yūgaku</i> tradition, referred to as "Music of the Left" (<i>sahō</i>). <i>Komagaku</i> was then referred to as "Music of the Right" (<i>uhō</i>), encompassing music influenced by both Korea (<i>sankangaku</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Balhae" title="Balhae">Balhae</a> (<i>bokkaigaku</i>). Though this division was prominent, it was not strict and the <i>tōgaku</i> and <i>komagaku</i> styles nonetheless interlaced and influenced each other.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarett2001"1._History"_158-3'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarett2001"1._History"-158'>[135]</a></sup> The long <a href="/wiki/Heian_period" title="Heian period">Heian period</a> (794–1185) saw much patronage of <i>gagaku</i> music from the court, as it accompanied many festivals and celebrations. Numerous new genres emerged at this time, such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Saibara" title="Saibara">saibara</a></i> and <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=R%C5%8Dei&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Rōei (page does not exist)">rōei</a></i> song forms.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarett2001"1._History"_158-4'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarett2001"1._History"-158'>[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarich-Schneider1957–1958183–185_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarich-Schneider1957–1958183–185-160">[136]</a></sup> <i>Gagaku</i> ensembles consist of a wide variety of instruments and are the largest such formations in <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Japanese_music" title="Traditional Japanese music">traditional Japanese music</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWade200524_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWade200524-161">[137]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_Europe">Medieval Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Medieval Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Medieval_music" title="Medieval music">Medieval music</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_medieval_composers" title="List of medieval composers">List of medieval composers</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_medieval_music_theorists" title="List of medieval music theorists">List of medieval music theorists</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_medieval_musical_instruments" title="List of medieval musical instruments">List of medieval musical instruments</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Perotin_-_Alleluia_nativitas.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="1400" decoding="async" height="377" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Perotin_-_Alleluia_nativitas.jpg/220px-Perotin_-_Alleluia_nativitas.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Perotin_-_Alleluia_nativitas.jpg/330px-Perotin_-_Alleluia_nativitas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Perotin_-_Alleluia_nativitas.jpg/440px-Perotin_-_Alleluia_nativitas.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Perotin_-_Alleluia_nativitas.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Alleluia nativitas</i> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perotin" title="Perotin">Perotin</a> from the Codex Guelf.1099</div></div></div>
<p>Modern scholars generally define '<a href="/wiki/Medieval_music" title="Medieval music">Medieval music</a>' as the music of <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>,<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolinskiBorders2020"Introduction"_162-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolinskiBorders2020"Introduction"-162'>[138]</a></sup> from approximately the 6th to 15th centuries. Music was certainly prominent in the <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a>, as attested by artistic depictions of instruments, writings about music, and other records; however, the only repertory of music which has survived from before 800 to the present day is the <a href="/wiki/Plainsong" title="Plainsong">plainsong</a> liturgical music of the Roman Catholic Church, the largest part of which is called <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_chant" title="Gregorian chant">Gregorian chant</a>. <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_I" title="Pope Gregory I">Pope Gregory I</a>, who gave his name to the musical repertory and may himself have been a composer, is usually claimed to be the originator of the musical portion of the liturgy in its present form, though the sources giving details on his contribution date from more than a hundred years after his death. Many scholars believe that his reputation has been exaggerated by legend. Most of the chant repertory was composed anonymously in the centuries between the time of Gregory and <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a>.
</p><p>During the 9th century, several important developments took place. First, there was a major effort by the Church to unify the many chant traditions and suppress many of them in favor of the Gregorian liturgy. Second, the earliest <a href="/wiki/Polyphony" title="Polyphony">polyphonic</a> music was sung, a form of parallel singing known as <a href="/wiki/Organum" title="Organum">organum</a>. Third, and of the greatest significance for music history, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Music_notation" title="Music notation">notation</a> was reinvented after a lapse of about five hundred years, though it would be several more centuries before a system of pitch and rhythm notation evolved having the precision and flexibility that modern musicians take for granted.
</p><p>Several schools of polyphony flourished in the period after 1100: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Martial_school" title="St. Martial school">St. Martial school</a> of organum, the music of which was often characterized by a swiftly moving part over a single sustained line; the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Notre_Dame_school" title="Notre Dame school">Notre Dame school</a> of polyphony, which included the composers <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9onin" title="Léonin">Léonin</a> and <a href="/wiki/P%C3%A9rotin" title="Pérotin">Pérotin</a>, and which produced the first music for more than two parts around 1200; the musical melting-pot of <a href="/wiki/Santiago_de_Compostela" title="Santiago de Compostela">Santiago de Compostela</a> in <a href="/wiki/Galicia_(Spain)" title="Galicia (Spain)">Galicia</a>, a pilgrimage destination and site where musicians from many traditions came together in the late Middle Ages, the music of whom survives in the <a href="/wiki/Codex_Calixtinus" title="Codex Calixtinus">Codex Calixtinus</a>; and the English school, the music of which survives in the <a href="/wiki/Worcester_Fragments" title="Worcester Fragments">Worcester Fragments</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Old_Hall_Manuscript" title="Old Hall Manuscript">Old Hall Manuscript</a>. Alongside these schools of sacred music a vibrant tradition of the secular song developed, as exemplified in the music of the <a href="/wiki/Troubadour" title="Troubadour">troubadours</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trouv%C3%A8re" title="Trouvère">trouvères</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minnesinger" title="Minnesinger">Minnesänger</a>. Much of the later secular music of the early <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> evolved from the forms, ideas, and the musical aesthetic of the troubadours, courtly poets, and itinerant musicians, whose culture was largely exterminated during the <a href="/wiki/Albigensian_Crusade" title="Albigensian Crusade">Albigensian Crusade</a> in the early 13th century.
</p><p>Forms of sacred music which developed during the late 13th century included the <a href="/wiki/Motet" title="Motet">motet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Conductus" title="Conductus">conductus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Discant" title="Discant">discant</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Clausula_(music)" title="Clausula (music)">clausulae</a>. One unusual development was the <i><a href="/wiki/Geisslerlieder" title="Geisslerlieder">Geisslerlieder</a></i>, the music of wandering bands of <a href="/wiki/Flagellant" title="Flagellant">flagellants</a> during two periods: the middle of the 13th century (until they were suppressed by the Church); and the period during and immediately following the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a>, around 1350, when their activities were vividly recorded and well-documented with notated music. Their music mixed folk song styles with penitential or apocalyptic texts. The 14th century in European music history is dominated by the style of the <i><a href="/wiki/Ars_nova" title="Ars nova">ars nova</a></i>, which by convention is grouped with the medieval era in music, even though it had much in common with early Renaissance ideals and <a href="/wiki/Aesthetics" title="Aesthetics">aesthetics</a>. Much of the surviving music of the time is secular, and tends to use the <a href="/wiki/Formes_fixes" title="Formes fixes">formes fixes</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Ballade_(forme_fixe)" title="Ballade (forme fixe)">ballade</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Virelai" title="Virelai">virelai</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Lai_(poetic_form)" title="Lai (poetic form)">lai</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rondeau_(music)" title="Rondeau (music)">rondeau</a>, which correspond to poetic forms of the same names. Most pieces in these forms are for one to three voices, likely with instrumental accompaniment: famous composers include <a href="/wiki/Guillaume_de_Machaut" title="Guillaume de Machaut">Guillaume de Machaut</a> and <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Landini" title="Francesco Landini">Francesco Landini</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Byzantine">Byzantine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Byzantine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_music" title="Byzantine music">Byzantine music</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Byzantine_composers" title="List of Byzantine composers">List of Byzantine composers</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Byzantine_music" title="Byzantine music">Prominent and diverse musical practices</a> were present in the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>, which existed by 395 to 1453.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[139]</a></sup> Both sacred and secular music were commonplace, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacred_music" title="Sacred music">sacred music</a> frequently used in church services and <a href="/wiki/Secular_music" title="Secular music">secular music</a> in many events including, ceremonies dramas, ballets, banquets, festivals and sports games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevy2001_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevy2001-164">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETouliatos2001_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETouliatos2001-165">[141]</a></sup> However, despite its popularity, secular Byzantine music was harshly criticized by the <a href="/wiki/Church_Fathers" title="Church Fathers">Church Fathers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETouliatos2001_165-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETouliatos2001-165">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[n 25]</a></sup> Composers of sacred music, especially hymns and chants, are generally well documented throughout the history of Byzantine music. However, those before the reign of <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian I</a> are virtually unknown; the monks Anthimos, Auxentios and Timokles are said to have written <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Troparia" title="Troparia">troparia</a>, but only the text to a single one by Auxentios survives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[142]</a></sup> The first major form was the <a href="/wiki/Kontakion" title="Kontakion">kontakion</a>, of which <a href="/wiki/Romanos_the_Melodist" title="Romanos the Melodist">Romanos the Melodist</a> was the foremost composer.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevy2001"10._Syllabic_hymn_settings:_(ii)_Kontakion"_168-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevy2001"10._Syllabic_hymn_settings:_(ii)_Kontakion"-168'>[143]</a></sup> In the late 7th century the <a href="/wiki/Canon_(hymnography)" title="Canon (hymnography)">kanōn</a> overtook the kontakion in popularity; <a href="/wiki/Andrew_of_Crete" title="Andrew of Crete">Andrew of Crete</a> became its first significant composer, and is traditionally credited as the genre's originator,<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevy2001"10._Syllabic_hymn_settings:_(iii)_Kanōn"_169-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevy2001"10._Syllabic_hymn_settings:_(iii)_Kanōn"-169'>[144]</a></sup> though modern scholars now doubt this attribution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[145]</a></sup> The kañon reached its peak with the music of <a href="/wiki/John_of_Damascus" title="John of Damascus">John of Damascus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cosmas_of_Maiuma" title="Cosmas of Maiuma">Cosmas of Maiuma</a> and later <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theodore_of_Stoudios" title="Theodore of Stoudios">Theodore of Stoudios</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theophanes_the_Branded" title="Theophanes the Branded">Theophanes the Branded</a> in the 8th and 9th centuries respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevy2001_164-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevy2001-164">[140]</a></sup> Composers of secular music are considerably less documented. Not until late in the empire's history are composers known by name, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joannes_Koukouzeles" title="Joannes Koukouzeles">Joannes Koukouzeles</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Xenos_Korones&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Xenos Korones (page does not exist)">Xenos Korones</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Joannes_Glykys&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Joannes Glykys (page does not exist)">Joannes Glykys</a> as the leading figures.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTETouliatos2001"3._Genres_and_composers."_171-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTETouliatos2001"3._Genres_and_composers."-171'>[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>Like their Western counterparts of the same period, Byzantine composers were primarily men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETouliatos-Banker198462_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETouliatos-Banker198462-172">[147]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kassia" title="Kassia">Kassia</a> is a major exception to this; she was a prolific and important composer of <a href="/wiki/Sticheron" title="Sticheron">sticheron</a> hymns and the only woman whose works entered the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_liturgy" title="Byzantine liturgy">Byzantine liturgy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMellas2020147–148_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMellas2020147–148-173">[148]</a></sup> A few other women are known to have been composers, Thekla, Theodosia, Martha and the daughter of <a href="/wiki/John_Kladas" title="John Kladas">John Kladas</a> (her given name is unrecorded).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETouliatos-Banker198480_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETouliatos-Banker198480-174">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[150]</a></sup> Only the latter has any surviving work, a single <a href="/wiki/Antiphon" title="Antiphon">antiphon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETouliatos-Banker198463_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETouliatos-Banker198463-176">[151]</a></sup> Some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_emperor" title="Byzantine emperor">Byzantine emperors</a> are known to have been composers, such as <a href="/wiki/Leo_VI_the_Wise" title="Leo VI the Wise">Leo VI the Wise</a> and <a href="/wiki/Constantine_VII" title="Constantine VII">Constantine VII</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_modern_and_modern_periods">Early modern and modern periods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Early modern and modern periods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indian_classical_music">Indian classical music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Indian classical music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Indian_classical_music" title="Indian classical music">Indian classical music</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_classical_music" title="Hindustani classical music">Hindustani classical music</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carnatic_classical_music" title="Carnatic classical music">Carnatic classical music</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Carnatic_composers" title="List of Carnatic composers">List of Carnatic composers</a></div>
<p>During the ancient and medieval periods, the <a href="/wiki/Art_music" title="Art music">classical music</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> was a largely unified practice. By the 14th century, socio-political turmoil inaugurated by the <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Sultanate" title="Delhi Sultanate">Delhi Sultanate</a> began to isolate Northern and Southern India, and independent traditions in each region began emerging. By the 16th-century two distinct styles had formed: the <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_classical_music" title="Hindustani classical music">Hindustani classical music</a> of the North and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carnatic_classical_music" title="Carnatic classical music">Carnatic classical music</a> of the South.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESorrellNarayan19803–4_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESorrellNarayan19803–4-179">[154]</a></sup> One of the major differences between them is that the Northern Hindustani vein was considerably influenced by the Persian and Arab musical practices of the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETe_Nijenhuis197480_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETe_Nijenhuis197480-180">[155]</a></sup> Carnatic music is largely devotional; the majority of the songs are addressed to the Hindu deities.
</p><p>Indian classical music (marga) is monophonic and based on a single melody line or <a href="/wiki/Raga" title="Raga">raga</a> rhythmically organized through <a href="/wiki/Tala_(music)" title="Tala (music)">talas</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Western_classical_music">Western classical music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Western classical music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_classical_music" title="Western classical music">Western classical music</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Renaissance">Renaissance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Renaissance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_music" title="Renaissance music">Renaissance music</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Renaissance_composers" title="List of Renaissance composers">List of Renaissance composers</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DufayBinchois.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="373" data-file-width="418" decoding="async" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/DufayBinchois.jpg/220px-DufayBinchois.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/DufayBinchois.jpg/330px-DufayBinchois.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4f/DufayBinchois.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DufayBinchois.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Guillaume_Du_Fay" title="Guillaume Du Fay">Guillaume Du Fay</a> (left), with <a href="/wiki/Gilles_Binchois" title="Gilles Binchois">Gilles Binchois</a> (right) in a <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1440</span> <a href="/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript" title="Illuminated manuscript">Illuminated manuscript</a> copy of <a href="/wiki/Martin_le_Franc" title="Martin le Franc">Martin le Franc's</a> <i>Le champion des dames</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[n 26]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The beginning of the Renaissance in music is not as clearly marked as the beginning of the Renaissance in the other arts, and unlike in the other arts, it did not begin in <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, but in northern Europe, specifically in the area currently comprising central and northern <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>. The style of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burgundian_school" title="Burgundian school">Burgundian</a> composers, as the first generation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dutch_School_(music)" title="Dutch School (music)">Franco-Flemish</a> school, is known, was at first a reaction against the excessive complexity and mannered style of the late 14th century <i><a href="/wiki/Ars_subtilior" title="Ars subtilior">ars subtilior</a></i>, and contained clear, singable melody and balanced <a href="/wiki/Polyphony" title="Polyphony">polyphony</a> in all voices. The most famous composers of the Burgundian school in the mid-15th century are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guillaume_Dufay" title="Guillaume Dufay">Guillaume Dufay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gilles_Binchois" title="Gilles Binchois">Gilles Binchois</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Busnois" title="Antoine Busnois">Antoine Busnois</a>.
</p><p>By the middle of the 15th century, composers and singers from the <a href="/wiki/Low_Countries" title="Low Countries">Low Countries</a> and adjacent areas began to spread across Europe, especially into Italy, where they were employed by the papal chapel and the aristocratic patrons of the arts (such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medici" title="Medici">Medici</a>, the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Este" title="House of Este">Este</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sforza" title="Sforza">Sforza</a> families). They carried their style with them: smooth polyphony which could be adapted for sacred or secular use as appropriate. Principal forms of sacred musical composition at the time were the <a href="/wiki/Mass_(music)" title="Mass (music)">mass</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Motet" title="Motet">motet</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laude" title="Laude">laude</a>; secular forms included the <a href="/wiki/Chanson" title="Chanson">chanson</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Frottola" title="Frottola">frottola</a>, and later the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Madrigal_(music)" title="Madrigal (music)">madrigal</a>.
</p><p>The invention of <a href="/wiki/Printing" title="Printing">printing</a> had an immense influence on the dissemination of musical styles, and along with the movement of the Franco-Flemish musicians, contributed to the establishment of the first truly international style in European music since the unification of Gregorian chant under Charlemagne.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Composers of the middle generation of the Franco-Flemish school included <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Ockeghem" title="Johannes Ockeghem">Johannes Ockeghem</a>, who wrote music in a contrapuntally complex style, with varied texture and an elaborate use of <a href="/wiki/Canon_(music)" title="Canon (music)">canonical</a> devices; <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Obrecht" title="Jacob Obrecht">Jacob Obrecht</a>, one of the most famous composers of masses in the last decades of the 15th century; and <a href="/wiki/Josquin_des_Prez" title="Josquin des Prez">Josquin des Prez</a>, probably the most famous composer in Europe before <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Pierluigi_da_Palestrina" title="Giovanni Pierluigi da Palestrina">Palestrina</a>, and who during the 16th century was renowned as one of the greatest artists in any form. Music in the generation after Josquin explored increasing complexity of <a href="/wiki/Counterpoint" title="Counterpoint">counterpoint</a>; possibly the most extreme expression is in the music of <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Gombert" title="Nicolas Gombert">Nicolas Gombert</a>, whose contrapuntal complexities influenced early instrumental music, such as the <a href="/wiki/Canzona" title="Canzona">canzona</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ricercar" title="Ricercar">ricercar</a>, ultimately culminating in <a href="/wiki/Baroque_music" title="Baroque music">Baroque</a> <a href="/wiki/Fugue" title="Fugue">fugal</a> forms.
</p><p>By the middle of the 16th century, the international style began to break down, and several highly diverse stylistic trends became evident: a trend towards simplicity in sacred music, as directed by the <a href="/wiki/Counter-Reformation" title="Counter-Reformation">Counter-Reformation</a> <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Trent" title="Council of Trent">Council of Trent</a>, exemplified in the music of <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Pierluigi_da_Palestrina" title="Giovanni Pierluigi da Palestrina">Giovanni Pierluigi da Palestrina</a>; a trend towards complexity and <a href="/wiki/Chromaticism" title="Chromaticism">chromaticism</a> in the madrigal, which reached its extreme expression in the <a href="/wiki/Avant-garde" title="Avant-garde">avant-garde</a> style of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferrara_School" title="Ferrara School">Ferrara School</a> of <a href="/wiki/Luzzasco_Luzzaschi" title="Luzzasco Luzzaschi">Luzzaschi</a> and the late century madrigalist <a href="/wiki/Carlo_Gesualdo" title="Carlo Gesualdo">Carlo Gesualdo</a>; and the grandiose, sonorous music of the <a href="/wiki/Venetian_School_(music)" title="Venetian School (music)">Venetian school</a>, which used the architecture of the Basilica <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/San_Marco_di_Venezia" title="San Marco di Venezia">San Marco di Venezia</a> to create <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polychoral" title="Polychoral">antiphonal</a> contrasts. The music of the Venetian school included the development of <a href="/wiki/Orchestration" title="Orchestration">orchestration</a>, ornamented instrumental parts, and <a href="/wiki/Basso_continuo" title="Basso continuo">continuo</a> bass parts, all of which occurred within a span of several decades around 1600. Famous composers in <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a> included the Gabrielis, <a href="/wiki/Andrea_Gabrieli" title="Andrea Gabrieli">Andrea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Gabrieli" title="Giovanni Gabrieli">Giovanni</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Claudio_Monteverdi" title="Claudio Monteverdi">Claudio Monteverdi</a>, one of the most significant innovators at the end of the era.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mensuralnotation-Missa_papae_marcelli.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1191" data-file-width="901" decoding="async" height="291" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Mensuralnotation-Missa_papae_marcelli.jpg/220px-Mensuralnotation-Missa_papae_marcelli.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Mensuralnotation-Missa_papae_marcelli.jpg/330px-Mensuralnotation-Missa_papae_marcelli.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Mensuralnotation-Missa_papae_marcelli.jpg/440px-Mensuralnotation-Missa_papae_marcelli.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mensuralnotation-Missa_papae_marcelli.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sheet music for part of the <i><a href="/wiki/Missa_Papae_Marcelli" title="Missa Papae Marcelli">Missa Papae Marcelli</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Pierluigi_da_Palestrina" title="Giovanni Pierluigi da Palestrina">Giovanni Pierluigi da Palestrina</a></div></div></div>
<p>Most parts of Europe had active and well-differentiated musical traditions by late in the century. In England, composers such as <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Tallis" title="Thomas Tallis">Thomas Tallis</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Byrd" title="William Byrd">William Byrd</a> wrote sacred music in a style similar to that written on the continent, while an active group of home-grown madrigalists adapted the Italian form for English tastes: famous composers included <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Morley" title="Thomas Morley">Thomas Morley</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Wilbye" title="John Wilbye">John Wilbye</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Weelkes" title="Thomas Weelkes">Thomas Weelkes</a>. Spain developed instrumental and vocal styles of its own, with <a href="/wiki/Tom%C3%A1s_Luis_de_Victoria" title="Tomás Luis de Victoria">Tomás Luis de Victoria</a> writing refined music similar to that of Palestrina, and numerous other composers writing for the new guitar. Germany cultivated polyphonic forms built on the Protestant <a href="/wiki/Chorale" title="Chorale">chorales</a>, which replaced the Roman Catholic Gregorian Chant as a basis for sacred music, and imported the style of the Venetian school (the appearance of which defined the start of the Baroque era there). In addition, German composers wrote enormous amounts of <a href="/wiki/Organ_(music)" title="Organ (music)">organ</a> music, establishing the basis for the later Baroque organ style which culminated in the work of <a href="/wiki/Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="Johann Sebastian Bach">J.S. Bach</a>. France developed a unique style of musical diction known as <a href="/wiki/Musique_mesur%C3%A9e" title="Musique mesurée">musique mesurée</a>, used in secular chansons, with composers such as <a href="/wiki/Guillaume_Costeley" title="Guillaume Costeley">Guillaume Costeley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Claude_Le_Jeune" title="Claude Le Jeune">Claude Le Jeune</a> prominent in the movement.
</p><p>One of the most revolutionary movements in the era took place in Florence in the 1570s and 1580s, with the work of the <a href="/wiki/Florentine_Camerata" title="Florentine Camerata">Florentine Camerata</a>, who ironically had a reactionary intent: dissatisfied with what they saw as contemporary musical depravities, their goal was to restore the music of the ancient Greeks. Chief among them were <a href="/wiki/Vincenzo_Galilei" title="Vincenzo Galilei">Vincenzo Galilei</a>, the father of the astronomer, and <a href="/wiki/Giulio_Caccini" title="Giulio Caccini">Giulio Caccini</a>. The fruits of their labors was a declamatory melodic singing style known as <a href="/wiki/Monody" title="Monody">monody</a>, and a corresponding staged dramatic form: a form known today as <a href="/wiki/Opera" title="Opera">opera</a>. The first operas, written around 1600, also define the end of the Renaissance and the beginning of the Baroque eras.
</p><p>Music prior to 1600 was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Musical_mode" title="Musical mode">modal</a> rather than <a href="/wiki/Tonality" title="Tonality">tonal</a>. Several theoretical developments late in the 16th century, such as the writings on scales on <a href="/wiki/Mode_(music)" title="Mode (music)">modes</a> by <a href="/wiki/Gioseffo_Zarlino" title="Gioseffo Zarlino">Gioseffo Zarlino</a> and <a href="/wiki/Franchinus_Gaffurius" title="Franchinus Gaffurius">Franchinus Gaffurius</a>, led directly to the development of common practice tonality. The major and minor scales began to predominate over the old <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Church_modes" title="Church modes">church modes</a>, a feature which was at first most obvious at cadential points in compositions, but gradually became pervasive. Music after 1600, beginning with the tonal music of the Baroque era, is often referred to as belonging to the <a href="/wiki/Common_practice_period" title="Common practice period">common practice period</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Baroque">Baroque</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Baroque">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Baroque_music" title="Baroque music">Baroque music</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Baroque_composers" title="List of Baroque composers">List of Baroque composers</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097025294">.mw-parser-output .side-box{margin:4px 0;box-sizing:border-box;border:1px solid #aaa;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;background-color:#f9f9f9}.mw-parser-output .side-box-abovebelow,.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{padding:0.25em 0.9em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-image{padding:2px 0 2px 0.9em;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-imageright{padding:2px 0.9em 2px 0;text-align:center}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .side-box-flex{display:flex;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{flex:1}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .side-box{width:238px}.mw-parser-output .side-box-right{clear:right;float:right;margin-left:1em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-left{margin-right:1em}}</style><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096940132">.mw-parser-output .listen .side-box-text{line-height:1.1em}.mw-parser-output .listen-plain{border:none;background:transparent}.mw-parser-output .listen-embedded{width:100%;margin:0;border-width:1px 0 0 0;background:transparent}.mw-parser-output .listen-header{padding:2px}.mw-parser-output .listen-embedded .listen-header{padding:2px 0}.mw-parser-output .listen-file-header{padding:4px 0}.mw-parser-output .listen .description{padding-top:2px}.mw-parser-output .listen .mw-tmh-player{max-width:100%}@media(max-width:719px){.mw-parser-output .listen{clear:both}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .listen:not(.listen-noimage){width:320px}.mw-parser-output .listen-left{overflow:visible;float:left}.mw-parser-output .listen-center{float:none;margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto}}</style><div class="side-box side-box-right listen noprint">
<div class="side-box-flex">

<div><audio controls="" data-durationhint="514" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Toccata_et_Fugue_BWV565.ogg" id="mwe_player_0" preload="none" style="width:232px;" width="232"><source data-bandwidth="95326" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (95 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/be/Toccata_et_Fugue_BWV565.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="196824" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/b/be/Toccata_et_Fugue_BWV565.ogg/Toccata_et_Fugue_BWV565.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/><track data-dir="ltr" kind="subtitles" label="English ‪(en)‬" src="https://commons.wikimedia.org/w/api.php?action=timedtext&amp;title=File%3AToccata_et_Fugue_BWV565.ogg&amp;lang=en&amp;trackformat=vtt&amp;origin=%2A" srclang="en" type="text/vtt"/></audio></div>
<div class="description">Performed by Ashtar Moïra</div></div></div></div>
<div class="side-box-abovebelow"><hr/><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></div>
</div>
<p>The Baroque era took place from 1600 to 1750, as the <a href="/wiki/Baroque" title="Baroque">Baroque artistic style</a> flourished across Europe and, during this time, music expanded in its range and complexity. Baroque music began when the first operas (dramatic solo vocal music accompanied by orchestra) were written. During the Baroque era, <a href="/wiki/Polyphony" title="Polyphony">polyphonic</a> <a href="/wiki/Counterpoint" title="Counterpoint">contrapuntal</a> music, in which multiple, simultaneous independent melody lines were used, remained important (counterpoint was important in the vocal music of the Medieval era).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (April 2018)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> German, Italian, French, Dutch, Polish, Spanish, Portuguese, and English Baroque composers wrote for small <a href="/wiki/Musical_ensemble" title="Musical ensemble">ensembles</a> including <a href="/wiki/String_section" title="String section">strings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brass_instrument" title="Brass instrument">brass</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Woodwind_instrument" title="Woodwind instrument">woodwinds</a>, as well as for <a href="/wiki/Choir" title="Choir">choirs</a> and keyboard instruments such as <a href="/wiki/Organ_(music)" title="Organ (music)">pipe organ</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harpsichord" title="Harpsichord">harpsichord</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Clavichord" title="Clavichord">clavichord</a>. During this period several major music forms were defined that lasted into later periods when they were expanded and evolved further, including the <a href="/wiki/Fugue" title="Fugue">fugue</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Invention_(musical_composition)" title="Invention (musical composition)">invention</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sonata" title="Sonata">sonata</a>, and the concerto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sdsu_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-sdsu-183">[157]</a></sup> The late Baroque style was polyphonically complex and richly ornamented. Important composers from the Baroque era include <a href="/wiki/Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="Johann Sebastian Bach">Johann Sebastian Bach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arcangelo_Corelli" title="Arcangelo Corelli">Arcangelo Corelli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Couperin" title="François Couperin">François Couperin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Girolamo_Frescobaldi" title="Girolamo Frescobaldi">Girolamo Frescobaldi</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Frideric_Handel" title="George Frideric Handel">George Frideric Handel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Lully" title="Jean-Baptiste Lully">Jean-Baptiste Lully</a>, <a href="/wiki/Claudio_Monteverdi" title="Claudio Monteverdi">Claudio Monteverdi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georg_Philipp_Telemann" title="Georg Philipp Telemann">Georg Philipp Telemann</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Vivaldi" title="Antonio Vivaldi">Antonio Vivaldi</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical">Classical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Classical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Classical_period_(music)" title="Classical period (music)">Classical period (music)</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Classical-era_composers" title="List of Classical-era composers">List of Classical-era composers</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner"
