srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Franz_Schubert_by_Wilhelm_August_Rieder_1875.jpg/194px-Franz_Schubert_by_Wilhelm_August_Rieder_1875.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Franz_Schubert_by_Wilhelm_August_Rieder_1875.jpg/258px-Franz_Schubert_by_Wilhelm_August_Rieder_1875.jpg 2x" width="129"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">The principal composers of the <a href="/wiki/Classical_period_(music)" title="Classical period (music)">Classical</a> period and the <a href="/wiki/Transition_from_Classical_to_Romantic_music" title="Transition from Classical to Romantic music">transition to Romanticism</a>, collectively known as the <a href="/wiki/First_Viennese_School" title="First Viennese School">First Viennese School</a>. Clockwise, from top left: <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Haydn" title="Joseph Haydn">Joseph Haydn</a> (<a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hardy_(English_painter)" title="Thomas Hardy (English painter)">Hardy</a>, 1791); <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Amadeus_Mozart" title="Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart">Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart</a> (<a href="/wiki/Johann_Nepomuk_della_Croce" title="Johann Nepomuk della Croce">della Croce</a>, 1781); <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_van_Beethoven" title="Ludwig van Beethoven">Ludwig van Beethoven</a> (<a href="/wiki/Joseph_Karl_Stieler" title="Joseph Karl Stieler">Stieler</a>, 1820); and <a href="/wiki/Franz_Schubert" title="Franz Schubert">Franz Schubert</a> (<a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_August_Rieder" title="Wilhelm August Rieder">Rieder</a>, 1875).</div></div></div></div>
<p>The music of the <a href="/wiki/Classical_period_(music)" title="Classical period (music)">Classical period</a> is characterized by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homophonic_texture" title="Homophonic texture">homophonic texture</a>, or an obvious <a href="/wiki/Melody" title="Melody">melody</a> with <a href="/wiki/Accompaniment" title="Accompaniment">accompaniment</a>. These new melodies tended to be almost voice-like and singable, allowing composers to actually replace singers as the focus of the music. Instrumental music therefore quickly replaced <a href="/wiki/Opera" title="Opera">opera</a> and other sung forms (such as <a href="/wiki/Oratorio" title="Oratorio">oratorio</a>) as the favorite of the musical audience and the epitome of great composition. However, opera did not disappear: during the classical period, several composers began producing operas for the general public in their native languages (previous operas were generally in Italian).
</p><p>Along with the gradual displacement of the voice in favor of stronger, clearer melodies, counterpoint also typically became a decorative flourish, often used near the end of a work or for a single <a href="/wiki/Movement_(music)" title="Movement (music)">movement</a>. In its stead, simple patterns, such as arpeggios and, in piano music, <a href="/wiki/Alberti_bass" title="Alberti bass">Alberti bass</a> (an accompaniment with a repeated pattern typically in the left hand), were used to liven the movement of the piece without creating a confusing additional voice. The now-popular instrumental music was dominated by several well-defined forms: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sonata_(music)" title="Sonata (music)">sonata</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Symphony" title="Symphony">symphony</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Concerto" title="Concerto">concerto</a>, though none of these were specifically defined or taught at the time as they are now in <a href="/wiki/Music_theory" title="Music theory">music theory</a>. All three derive from <a href="/wiki/Sonata_form" title="Sonata form">sonata form</a>, which is both the overlying form of an entire work and the structure of a single movement. Sonata form matured during the Classical era to become the primary form of instrumental compositions throughout the 19th century.
</p><p>The early Classical period was ushered in by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mannheim_School" title="Mannheim School">Mannheim School</a>, which included such composers as <a href="/wiki/Johann_Stamitz" title="Johann Stamitz">Johann Stamitz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Franz_Xaver_Richter" title="Franz Xaver Richter">Franz Xaver Richter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Stamitz" title="Carl Stamitz">Carl Stamitz</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Christian_Cannabich" title="Christian Cannabich">Christian Cannabich</a>. It exerted a profound influence on <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Haydn" title="Joseph Haydn">Joseph Haydn</a> and, through him, on all subsequent European music. <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Amadeus_Mozart" title="Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart">Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart</a> was the central figure of the Classical period, and his phenomenal and varied output in all genres defines our perception of the period. <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_van_Beethoven" title="Ludwig van Beethoven">Ludwig van Beethoven</a> and <a href="/wiki/Franz_Schubert" title="Franz Schubert">Franz Schubert</a> were transitional composers, leading into the Romantic period, with their expansion of existing genres, forms, and even functions of music.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Romantic">Romantic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Romantic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Romantic_music" title="Romantic music">Romantic music</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Romantic_composers" title="List of Romantic composers">List of Romantic composers</a></div>
<p>In the Romantic period, music became more expressive and emotional, expanding to encompass literature, art, and philosophy. Famous early Romantic composers include <a href="/wiki/Robert_Schumann" title="Robert Schumann">Schumann</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Chopin" title="Frédéric Chopin">Chopin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Felix_Mendelssohn" title="Felix Mendelssohn">Mendelssohn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vincenzo_Bellini" title="Vincenzo Bellini">Bellini</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gaetano_Donizetti" title="Gaetano Donizetti">Donizetti</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hector_Berlioz" title="Hector Berlioz">Berlioz</a>. The late 19th century saw a dramatic expansion in the size of the <a href="/wiki/Orchestra" title="Orchestra">orchestra</a>, and in the role of concerts as part of <a href="/wiki/Urban_culture" title="Urban culture">urban</a> society. Famous composers from the second half of the century include <a href="/wiki/Johann_Strauss_II" title="Johann Strauss II">Johann Strauss II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Brahms" title="Johannes Brahms">Brahms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Franz_Liszt" title="Franz Liszt">Liszt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pyotr_Ilyich_Tchaikovsky" title="Pyotr Ilyich Tchaikovsky">Tchaikovsky</a>, <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Verdi" title="Giuseppe Verdi">Verdi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Wagner" title="Richard Wagner">Wagner</a>. Between 1890 and 1910, a third wave of composers including <a href="/wiki/Edvard_Grieg" title="Edvard Grieg">Grieg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anton%C3%ADn_Dvo%C5%99%C3%A1k" title="Antonín Dvořák">Dvořák</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Mahler" title="Gustav Mahler">Mahler</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Strauss" title="Richard Strauss">Richard Strauss</a>, <a href="/wiki/Giacomo_Puccini" title="Giacomo Puccini">Puccini</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jean_Sibelius" title="Jean Sibelius">Sibelius</a> built on the work of middle Romantic composers to create even more complex – and often much longer – musical works. A prominent mark of late 19th-century music is its nationalistic fervor, as exemplified by such figures as Dvořák, Sibelius, and Grieg. Other prominent late-century figures include <a href="/wiki/Camille_Saint-Sa%C3%ABns" title="Camille Saint-Saëns">Saint-Saëns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gabriel_Faur%C3%A9" title="Gabriel Fauré">Fauré</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sergei_Rachmaninoff" title="Sergei Rachmaninoff">Rachmaninoff</a>, <a href="/wiki/C%C3%A9sar_Franck" title="César Franck">Franck</a>, <a href="/wiki/Claude_Debussy" title="Claude Debussy">Debussy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nikolai_Rimsky-Korsakov" title="Nikolai Rimsky-Korsakov">Rimsky-Korsakov</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_and_21st_century">20th and 21st century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: 20th and 21st century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/20th-century_music" title="20th-century music">20th-century music</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/20th-century_classical_music" title="20th-century classical music">20th-century classical music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Modernism_(music)" title="Modernism (music)">Modernism (music)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_classical_music" title="Contemporary classical music">Contemporary classical music</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Popular_music" title="Popular music">Popular music</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_modernist_composers" title="List of modernist composers">List of modernist composers</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_postmodernist_composers" title="List of postmodernist composers">List of postmodernist composers</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_20th-century_classical_composers" title="List of 20th-century classical composers">List of 20th-century classical composers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_21st-century_classical_composers" title="List of 21st-century classical composers">List of 21st-century classical composers</a></div>
<p>The 20th century saw a revolution in music listening as the <a href="/wiki/Radio" title="Radio">radio</a> gained popularity worldwide and new media and technologies were developed to record, edit and distribute music. Music performances became increasingly visual with the broadcast and recording of performances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p>20th-century music brought new freedom and wide experimentation with new musical styles and forms that challenged the accepted rules of music of earlier periods.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The invention of musical <a href="/wiki/Amplifier" title="Amplifier">amplification</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electronic_instrument" title="Electronic instrument">electronic instruments</a>, especially the synthesizer, in the mid-20th century revolutionized classical and popular music, and accelerated the development of new forms of music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[159]</a></sup>
</p><p>As for classical music, two fundamental schools determined the course of the century: that of <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Schoenberg" title="Arnold Schoenberg">Arnold Schoenberg</a> and that of <a href="/wiki/Igor_Stravinsky" title="Igor Stravinsky">Igor Stravinsky</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[160]</a></sup> However, other composers also had a notable influence. For example: <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A9la_Bart%C3%B3k" title="Béla Bartók">Béla Bartók</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anton_Webern" title="Anton Webern">Anton Webern</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dmitri_Shostakovich" title="Dmitri Shostakovich">Dmitri Shostakovich</a>, <a href="/wiki/Olivier_Messiaen" title="Olivier Messiaen">Olivier Messiaen</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Cage" title="John Cage">John Cage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Britten" title="Benjamin Britten">Benjamin Britten</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karlheinz_Stockhausen" title="Karlheinz Stockhausen">Karlheinz Stockhausen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sofia_Gubaidulina" title="Sofia Gubaidulina">Sofia Gubaidulina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Krzysztof_Penderecki" title="Krzysztof Penderecki">Krzysztof Penderecki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brian_Ferneyhough" title="Brian Ferneyhough">Brian Ferneyhough</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kaija_Saariaho" title="Kaija Saariaho">Kaija Saariaho</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[161]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 20th century saw the unprecedented dissemination of <a href="/wiki/Popular_music" title="Popular music">popular music</a>, that is, music with a wide appeal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[n 27]</a></sup> The term has its roots in the music of the American <a href="/wiki/Tin_Pan_Alley" title="Tin Pan Alley">Tin Pan Alley</a>, a group of prominent musicians and publishers who began to emerge during the 1880s in New York City. Although popular music sometimes is known as "pop music", the two terms are not interchangeable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[163]</a></sup> Popular music is a generic term for a wide variety of genres of music that appeal to the tastes of a large segment of the population,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[164]</a></sup> whereas <a href="/wiki/Pop_music" title="Pop music">pop music</a> usually refers to a specific musical genre <i>within</i> popular music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[165]</a></sup> Popular music songs and pieces typically have easily singable <a href="/wiki/Melody" title="Melody">melodies</a>. The song structure of popular music commonly involves repetition of sections, with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Verse_(popular_music)" title="Verse (popular music)">verse</a> and chorus or <a href="/wiki/Refrain" title="Refrain">refrain</a> repeating throughout the song and the <a href="/wiki/Bridge_(music)" title="Bridge (music)">bridge</a> providing a contrasting and transitional section within a piece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-:7-193">[166]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-columns references-column-width" style="column-width: 30em;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Such coupling dates back to at least the 18th century of the European <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a>, with philosopher <a href="/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Rousseau" title="Jean-Jacques Rousseau">Jean-Jacques Rousseau</a>'s posthumous 1781 <i><a href="/wiki/Essay_on_the_Origin_of_Languages" title="Essay on the Origin of Languages">Essay on the Origin of Languages</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMithen200514_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMithen200514-8">[8]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/Soci%C3%A9t%C3%A9_de_Linguistique_de_Paris" title="Société de Linguistique de Paris">Société de Linguistique de Paris</a> banned discussion on the origin of language in 1866 and similar dismissal surrounded most of the linguist community into the mid-20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMithen200513_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMithen200513-10">[9]</a></sup> The editors of the monumental interdisciplinary volume <i>The Origins of Music</i> (2000) note that "musicology did not seem to need an official decree [...] to make the topic of music origins unfashionable among musicologists"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWallinMerkerBrown20003_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWallinMerkerBrown20003-12">[11]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In a similar fashion, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biomusicologist" title="Biomusicologist">biomusicologist</a> Steven Brown differentiates theories on the topic in two ways: "structural models", which see music as an outgrowth of preexisting abilities, and "functional models" which consider its emergence as an adaptive technique.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown2000271_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown2000271-15">[13]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A well-known promoter of the spandrel view is the cognitive psychologist and linguist <a href="/wiki/Steven_Pinker" title="Steven Pinker">Steven Pinker</a>; in <i><a href="/wiki/How_the_Mind_Works" title="How the Mind Works">How the Mind Works</a></i> (1997), Pinker famously referred to music as "auditory cheesecake”,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPinker1997534_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPinker1997534-17">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMithen200518_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMithen200518-18">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevitinTirovolas2009212–213_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevitinTirovolas2009212–213-19">[16]</a></sup> since it is "a technology, not an adaptation",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPinker1997529_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPinker1997529-20">[17]</a></sup> and because, "as far as biological cause and effect is concerned, music is useless." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPinker1997528_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPinker1997528-21">[18]</a></sup> Scholars such as <a href="/wiki/John_D._Barrow" title="John D. Barrow">John D. Barrow</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dan_Sperber" title="Dan Sperber">Dan Sperber</a> have agreed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECross200349_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECross200349-22">[19]</a></sup> and the music psychologist <a href="/wiki/Sandra_Trehub" title="Sandra Trehub">Sandra Trehub</a> noted that like Pinker, "much of the larger scientific community is highly skeptical about links between music and biology", in opposition to many specialists on the subjects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrehub20033_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrehub20033-23">[20]</a></sup> Other scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Carroll_(scholar)" title="Joseph Carroll (scholar)">Joseph Carroll</a> and Anna K. Tirovolas rejected Pinker's take, suggesting evolutionary advantages such as music's use as practice for cognitive flexibility and <a href="/wiki/Courtship_display" title="Courtship display">courtship display</a>, particularly its ability to demonstrate one's "cognitive and physical flexibility and fitness".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevitinTirovolas2009213_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevitinTirovolas2009213-24">[21]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-28">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Many fields that developed developed significantly or begun in the mid-late 20th century discuss and study the origins of music to some extent. These include <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_musicology" title="Evolutionary musicology">evolutionary musicology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Music_archaeology" title="Music archaeology">music archaeology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biomusicology" title="Biomusicology">biomusicology</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cognitive_neuroscience_of_music" title="Cognitive neuroscience of music">neuromusicology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethnomusicology" title="Ethnomusicology">comparative musicology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorley20132–3_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorley20132–3-26">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWallinMerkerBrown20005–6_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWallinMerkerBrown20005–6-27">[23]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-44">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For an alternate story on the origin of music in Chinese mythology, see a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-subscription a{background:linear-gradient(transparent,transparent),url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/aa/Lock-red-alt-2.svg")right 0.1em center/9px </li>
<li id="cite_note-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-46">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For further information on the story of Ling Lun, see <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1 cs1-prop-long-vol" id="CITEREFKárpáti1980">Kárpáti, János (1980). "Evolution of Primate Cognition". <i>Studia Musicologica Academiae Scientiarum Hungaricae</i>. <a href="/wiki/Akad%C3%A9miai_Kiad%C3%B3" title="Akadémiai Kiadó">Akadémiai Kiadó</a>. T. 22 (Facsimile 1/4): 5. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.2307%2F901989" rel="nofollow">10.2307/901989</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/JSTOR_(identifier)" title="JSTOR (identifier)">JSTOR</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.jstor.org/stable/901989" rel="nofollow">901989</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Studia+Musicologica+Academiae+Scientiarum+Hungaricae&amp;rft.atitle=Evolution+of+Primate+Cognition&amp;rft.volume=T.+22&amp;rft.issue=Facsimile+1%2F4&amp;rft.pages=5&amp;rft.date=1980&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.2307%2F901989&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.jstor.org%2Fstable%2F901989%23id-name%3DJSTOR&amp;rft.aulast=K%C3%A1rp%C3%A1ti&amp;rft.aufirst=J%C3%A1nos&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHistory+of+music"></span> The tale is recorded earliest in the <i><a href="/wiki/L%C3%BCshi_Chunqiu" title="Lüshi Chunqiu">Lüshi Chunqiu</a></i>;<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalm2019"Ancient_artifacts_and_writings"_45-1'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalm2019"Ancient_artifacts_and_writings"-45'>[39]</a></sup> for an English translation of that account, see <a href="#CITEREFDewoskin1982">Dewoskin (1982</a>, pp. 59–60)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-51">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Though the term "primitive music" includes extinct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Literate" title="Literate">preliterate</a> cultures, it also refers to existing <a href="/wiki/Uncontacted_peoples" title="Uncontacted peoples">uncontacted</a> <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples" title="Indigenous peoples">indigenous peoples</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENettl19561–5_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENettl19561–5-49">[42]</a></sup> The term is now outdated, though <a href="#CITEREFWallinMerkerBrown2000">Wallin, Merker &amp; Brown (2000</a>, p. 21) assert that the 20th-century musicologists that used it had "nothing less but respect" for the music in question.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWallinMerkerBrown200021_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWallinMerkerBrown200021-50">[43]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-59"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-59">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/Haua_Fteah" title="Haua Fteah">Haua Fteah</a> cave, Libya; <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ilsenh%C3%B6hle&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ilsenhöhle (page does not exist)">Ilsenhöhle</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ilsenh%C3%B6hle" title="de:Ilsenhöhle">de</a>]</span>, Germany; and <a href="/wiki/Kents_Cavern" title="Kents Cavern">Kents Cavern</a>, England each contain an object that has been proposed as a Middle Paleolithic flute, though none have achieved wide scholarly acceptance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorley201338_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorley201338-57">[49]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See <a href="#CITEREFMorley2013">Morley (2013</a>, pp. 43–45) for a comprehensive table on all eight surviving flutes from the <a href="/wiki/Geissenkl%C3%B6sterle" title="Geissenklösterle">Geissenklösterle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hohle_Fels" title="Hohle Fels">Hohle Fels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vogelherd_Cave" title="Vogelherd Cave">Vogelherd</a> caves</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-69"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-69">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">These bone flutes (<i>gudi</i>) were made from the <a href="/wiki/Red-crowned_crane" title="Red-crowned crane">red-crowned crane</a>; they are perhaps the earliest instruments from anywhere in the world that remain playable.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2009"Archaeological_Evidence_of_Musical_Instruments"_68-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2009"Archaeological_Evidence_of_Musical_Instruments"-68'>[58]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-70"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-70">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The next oldest are various bone whistles in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemudu" title="Hemudu">Hemudu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yuyao" title="Yuyao">Yuyao</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhejiang_Province" title="Zhejiang Province">Zhejiang Province</a> from <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 5000</span> BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuangsheng200088,_91_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuangsheng200088,_91-67">[57]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-89"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-89">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As of 1983, <a href="#CITEREFTong1983a">Tong (1983a</a>, p. 79) notes that "several dozen Shang <i>qing</i> have been unearthed in [the 20th century]". From what is known, the common people did not use <i>qing</i> during the Shang dynasty (1600–1046); many <i>qing</i> instruments have decorations akin to the bronzeware and jade items of the Shang elite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETong1983a111_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETong1983a111-88">[76]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-93"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-93">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1300–1050</span> BCE date for <i>paixiao</i> is from pictographic evidence that the oracle script the character for <i>he</i> (attunement) is a pan flute—but is only securely documented in 700 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuangsheng200092_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuangsheng200092-92">[79]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-98"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-98">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">From all available evidence, <a href="/wiki/String_instrument" title="String instrument">string instruments</a> do not appear to have existed in the <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESoMajor200033_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESoMajor200033-84">[72]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-101"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-101">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <i><a href="/wiki/Guqin" title="Guqin">guqin</a></i> (or <i>qin</i>) may have been of Northern, non-Chinese origin,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESoMajor200030–32_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESoMajor200030–32-99">[84]</a></sup> but it still had a massive impact of future Chinese music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MET2_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-MET2-100">[85]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-109"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-109">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The monumental <i><a href="/wiki/Records_of_the_Grand_Historian" title="Records of the Grand Historian">Records of the Grand Historian</a></i> of the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> denounces the last king of the Shang, <a href="/wiki/King_Zhou_of_Shang" title="King Zhou of Shang">King Zhou</a>, declaring that "he loved wine and licentious music... thus he had Shi Zhuan create new and depraved sounds... the 'Fluttering Earthwards' music",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESoMajor200025–26_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESoMajor200025–26-107">[91]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/King_Wu_of_Zhou" title="King Wu of Zhou">King Wu</a> purportedly used <a href="/wiki/Pitch_pipe" title="Pitch pipe">pitch pipes</a> to foresee the outcome in his war against King Zhou.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuangsheng200090_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuangsheng200090-108">[92]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-117"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-117">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In a fuller form, the theories of <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">ganying</i></span> held that the universe was governed by natural, invisible and "resonant" forces, including <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Qi" title="Qi">qi</a></i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Yin_and_yang" title="Yin and yang">yin and yang</a> and music, which according to the <a href="/wiki/Record_of_Music" title="Record of Music"><i>Yueji</i> Record of Music</a> "represents the attunement of Heaven and Earth."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrindley20123–5_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrindley20123–5-115">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESoMajor200029_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESoMajor200029-116">[99]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-122"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-122">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Ancient texts attribute the invention of the <i><a href="/wiki/Guzheng" title="Guzheng">guzheng</a></i> to the Qin as well, but earlier specimens have been found from the 5th-century BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESoMajor200019_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESoMajor200019-83">[71]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-125"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-125">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">There may have been an incipient form of the <a href="/wiki/Music_Bureau" title="Music Bureau">Music Bureau</a> (<span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">Yuefu</i></span>) during the Qin dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[105]</a></sup> In general, the chronology of the <a href="/wiki/Music_Bureau" title="Music Bureau">Music Bureau</a> is unclear and extremely contradictory in ancient records. See <a href="#CITEREFBirrell1993">Birrell (1993</a>, pp. xviii–xx) for further information</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-126"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-126">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See <a href="#CITEREFBirrell1993">Birrell (1993</a>, pp. xxi–xxiv) for information on the location and amount of surviving folksongs.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-141"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-141">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The size of the trumpets is so much smaller than modern trumpets that some scholars suggest they were actually funnels or spouts, but Lawergren maintains that such interpretations are "unlikely considering the precious materials and the extraordinary workmanship".<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTELawergren2001"2._3rd_millennium_BCE:_(iii)_Trumpets"_140-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawergren2001"2._3rd_millennium_BCE:_(iii)_Trumpets"-140'>[119]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-151"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-151">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Āzādvar-e Changi is also known as simply Āzād.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFarhat20043_147-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFarhat20043-147">[125]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-159"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-159">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The musicologist Allan Marett noted, however, that by the time of <i><a href="/wiki/Gagaku" title="Gagaku">gagaku</a></i> music in the early 8th century, contemporary Chinese <i>yayue</i> music had adopted wide influences and genres, making it similar to the stylistic-variety of <i>gagaku</i>.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarett2001"1._History"_158-1'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarett2001"1._History"-158'>[135]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-166"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-166">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFTouliatos2001">Touliatos 2001</a> lists <a href="/wiki/John_Chrysostom" title="John Chrysostom">John Chrysostom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tertullian" title="Tertullian">Tertullian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyprian" title="Cyprian">Cyprian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clement_of_Alexandria" title="Clement of Alexandria">Clement of Alexandria</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gregory_Nazianzen" title="Gregory Nazianzen">Gregory Nazianzen</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basil_the_Great" title="Basil the Great">Basil the Great</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ambrose" title="Ambrose">Ambrose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a> and particularly <a href="/wiki/Jerome" title="Jerome">Jerome</a> as the church fathers who were vocally against secular Byzantine music</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-182"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-182">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The illuminator of this depiction likely knew Du Fay and it is the best known of two surviving depictions of him. The other is carving on Du Fay's funeral monument where he is kneeling in the bottom left corner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPlanchart2001_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlanchart2001-181">[156]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-189"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-189">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In an essay on popular music's history for <i><a href="/wiki/Collier%27s_Encyclopedia" title="Collier's Encyclopedia">Collier's Encyclopedia</a></i> (1984), <a href="/wiki/Robert_Christgau" title="Robert Christgau">Robert Christgau</a> explained, "Some sort of popular music has existed for as long as there has been an urban <a href="/wiki/Middle_class" title="Middle class">middle class</a> to consume it. What distinguishes it above all is the aesthetic level it is aimed at. The cultural elite has always endowed music with an exalted if not self-important religious or aesthetic status, while for the rural folk, it has been practical and unselfconscious, an accompaniment to fieldwork or to the festivals that provide a periodic escape from toil. But since Rome and Alexandria, professional entertainers have diverted and edified city dwellers with songs, marches, and dances, whose pretensions fell somewhere in between."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[162]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div>
